{"monkeypoxnum_articles": "137", "monkeypox": [{"archive_id": "8142516", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa (01): Congo DR 2020", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=8142516", "date": "2020-02-18 15:33:24", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (04): DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO, WHO\n**********************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Sun 16 Feb 2020, 5:00 PM\nSource: WHO, Weekly Bulletin on Outbreaks and Other Emergencies, page 9 [abridged, edited]\nhttps://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/handle/10665/331023/OEW07-1016022020.pdf\n\n\nDuring week 4 [week ending 26 Jan 2020], a total of 73 suspected cases including one death were reported across the country, compared to 46 suspected cases and no deaths in the previous week. The majority of cases in week 4 were reported from Sankuru province (78%).\n\nIn the past 4 weeks (weeks 1 to 4 of 2020) a total of 222 suspected cases with 4 deaths (CFR: 1.8%) were notified in the country, with the majority of cases being reported from the provinces of Sankuru (31%), Bas-Uele (18%), Equateur (15%) and Mai-Ndombe (9%). There has been an increase in the weekly case incidence since week 2 of 2020.\n\nBetween weeks 1 and 52 of 2019 a cumulative total of 5288 monkeypox cases, including 107 deaths (CFR 2%) were reported from 133 health zones in 19 provinces.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Monkeypox (MPX) virus is endemic and widespread geographically in the DR Congo, with cases occurring sporadically in several provinces. January 2020 is off to a similar start with 222 suspected cases and 4 deaths reported in 4 provinces. The 2% CFR is relatively low for the clade of MPX that occurs in the DRC, which can reach 10% or more. The 222 cases are suspected, and there is the possibility that without laboratory confirmation some of them may be varicella cases misdiagnosed as MPX. There is no additional information about the circumstances under which these cases acquired their infection. Monkeys are not the reservoirs of the virus, despite the name that the virus has received. Studies of prevalence of MPX virus in populations of rodent hosts are not mentioned in this or previous reports. The main reservoirs of MPX virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp., an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_. Halting the bushmeat trade and consumption of wild animals to halt MPX virus exposure will be culturally and economically difficult, so continued occurrence of cases can be expected. MPX virus can be transmitted between people but not readily. - Mod.TY\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nDR Congo: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/194]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (01): Congo DR 2019, WHO 20200102.6868194\n2019\n----\nMonkeypox - Africa (03): Congo DR, Republic of the Congo 20190409.6413379\n2018\n----\nMonkeypox - Africa (04) 20180327.5712702\nMonkeypox - Africa (04): Congo DR, Central African Republic 20170705.5151556\n.................................................sb/jh/ty/ao/jh", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "varicella", "monkeypox"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2020-02-16 00:00:00", "2020-01-26 00:00:00", "5288-03-16 00:00:00", "2020-01-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2019-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-03 00:00:00", "2018-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "the DR Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Sankuru", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo DR", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "7829916", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa (08): DRC, WHO", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=7829916", "date": "2020-02-18 15:33:24", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (04): DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO, WHO\n**********************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Sun 16 Feb 2020, 5:00 PM\nSource: WHO, Weekly Bulletin on Outbreaks and Other Emergencies, page 9 [abridged, edited]\nhttps://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/handle/10665/331023/OEW07-1016022020.pdf\n\n\nDuring week 4 [week ending 26 Jan 2020], a total of 73 suspected cases including one death were reported across the country, compared to 46 suspected cases and no deaths in the previous week. January 2020 is off to a similar start with 222 suspected cases and 4 deaths reported in 4 provinces. The 2% CFR is relatively low for the clade of MPX that occurs in the DRC, which can reach 10% or more. The 222 cases are suspected, and there is the possibility that without laboratory confirmation some of them may be varicella cases misdiagnosed as MPX. There is no additional information about the circumstances under which these cases acquired their infection. Monkeys are not the reservoirs of the virus, despite the name that the virus has received. The main reservoirs of MPX virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp., an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_. Halting the bushmeat trade and consumption of wild animals to halt MPX virus exposure will be culturally and economically difficult, so continued occurrence of cases can be expected. MPX virus can be transmitted between people but not readily. - Mod.TY\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nDR Congo: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/194]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (01): Congo DR 2019, WHO 20200102.6868194\n2019\n----\nMonkeypox - Africa (03): Congo DR, Republic of the Congo 20190409.6413379\n2018\n----\nMonkeypox - Africa (04) 20180327.5712702\nMonkeypox - Africa (04): Congo DR, Central African Republic 20170705.5151556\n.................................................sb/jh/ty/ao/jh", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "varicella", "monkeypox"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2020-02-16 00:00:00", "2020-01-26 00:00:00", "5288-03-16 00:00:00", "2020-01-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2019-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-03 00:00:00", "2018-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "the DR Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Sankuru", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo DR", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "7740114", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa (07): Congo DR (SN)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=7740114", "date": "2020-02-18 15:33:24", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (04): DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO, WHO\n**********************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Sun 16 Feb 2020, 5:00 PM\nSource: WHO, Weekly Bulletin on Outbreaks and Other Emergencies, page 9 [abridged, edited]\nhttps://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/handle/10665/331023/OEW07-1016022020.pdf\n\n\nDuring week 4 [week ending 26 Jan 2020], a total of 73 suspected cases including one death were reported across the country, compared to 46 suspected cases and no deaths in the previous week. The majority of cases in week 4 were reported from Sankuru province (78%).\n\nIn the past 4 weeks (weeks 1 to 4 of 2020) a total of 222 suspected cases with 4 deaths (CFR: 1.8%) were notified in the country, with the majority of cases being reported from the provinces of Sankuru (31%), Bas-Uele (18%), Equateur (15%) and Mai-Ndombe (9%). There has been an increase in the weekly case incidence since week 2 of 2020.\n\nBetween weeks 1 and 52 of 2019 a cumulative total of 5288 monkeypox cases, including 107 deaths (CFR 2%) were reported from 133 health zones in 19 provinces.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Monkeypox (MPX) virus is endemic and widespread geographically in the DR Congo, with cases occurring sporadically in several provinces. January 2020 is off to a similar start with 222 suspected cases and 4 deaths reported in 4 provinces. The 2% CFR is relatively low for the clade of MPX that occurs in the DRC, which can reach 10% or more. The 222 cases are suspected, and there is the possibility that without laboratory confirmation some of them may be varicella cases misdiagnosed as MPX. There is no additional information about the circumstances under which these cases acquired their infection. Monkeys are not the reservoirs of the virus, despite the name that the virus has received. Studies of prevalence of MPX virus in populations of rodent hosts are not mentioned in this or previous reports. The main reservoirs of MPX virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp., an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_. Halting the bushmeat trade and consumption of wild animals to halt MPX virus exposure will be culturally and economically difficult, so continued occurrence of cases can be expected. MPX virus can be transmitted between people but not readily. - Mod.TY\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nDR Congo: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/194]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (01): Congo DR 2019, WHO 20200102.6868194\n2019\n----\nMonkeypox - Africa (03): Congo DR, Republic of the Congo 20190409.6413379\n2018\n----\nMonkeypox - Africa (04) 20180327.5712702\nMonkeypox - Africa (04): Congo DR, Central African Republic 20170705.5151556\n.................................................sb/jh/ty/ao/jh", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "varicella", "monkeypox"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2020-02-16 00:00:00", "2020-01-26 00:00:00", "5288-03-16 00:00:00", "2020-01-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2019-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-03 00:00:00", "2018-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "the DR Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Sankuru", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo DR", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "6868194", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa (01): Congo DR 2019, WHO", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6868194", "date": "2019-05-11 12:00:16", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - SINGAPORE ex NIGERIA\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 9 May 2019\nSource: Straits Times [edited]\nhttps://www.straitstimes.com/singapore/moh-confirms-one-imported-case-of-monkeypox-in-singapore\n\n\nThe Ministry of Health (MOH) has confirmed one imported case of monkeypox infection in Singapore, involving a Nigerian national who arrived last month [April 2019] for a workshop. The 38-year-old man tested positive for the rare viral disease, which is primarily transmitted to humans from animals, on Wednesday (8 May [2019]), said the MOH on Thursday [9 May 2019]. It is the 1st case of monkeypox reported here.\n\nHe is currently in an isolation ward at the National Centre for Infectious Diseases (NCID), and is in stable condition.\n\nMeanwhile, 22 of 23 individuals who have been identified as close contacts of the patient are also under quarantine as a precautionary measure. One of the close contacts, a workshop participant, had left Singapore on 5 May [2019] before the patient was diagnosed. He has reported to the MOH that he remains well with no symptoms.\n\n\"Nonetheless, as a precaution, MOH has informed the public health authority in his home country,\" said the ministry. The MOH added that while it is possible for the disease to be transmitted between humans, the risk of spread is low.\n\nThe MOH offers the following precautions to take when travelling to areas affected by monkeypox in central and western Africa:\n- Maintain a high standard of personal hygiene, including frequent hand washing after going to the toilet, or when hands are soiled.\n- Avoid direct contact with skin lesions of infected living or dead persons or animals, as well as objects that may have become contaminated with infectious fluids, such as soiled clothing or linens (e.g., bedding or towels) used by an infected person.\n- Avoid contact with wild animals, and consumption of bush meat.\n- Returning travellers from areas affected by monkeypox should seek immediate medical attention if they develop any disease symptoms (e.g., sudden onset of high fever, swollen lymph nodes and rash) within 3 weeks of their return. They should inform their doctor of their recent travel history.\n\nBefore coming to Singapore, the man attended a wedding in Nigeria, where he may have consumed bush meat -- a possible source of transmission of the monkeypox virus. Bush meat, the meat of wild animals hunted and sold for food, is a popular source of protein in some parts of Africa where meat from domesticated animals is scarce or expensive.\n\nUpon his arrival on 28 Apr [2019], he stayed at Hotel 81 Orchid in Geylang. In the following 2 days, he attended a workshop at the Samsung Hub in Church Street, in the Central Business District.\n\nOn 30 Apr [2019], he developed fever, muscle ache, chills and skin rash. Between 1 and 7 May [2019], the man reported that he stayed in his hotel room most of the time. On 7 May [2019], he was taken to Tan Tock Seng Hospital by ambulance and referred to NCID.\n\nSubsequently, the MOH identified the people who had come into close contact with the man, including 18 participants and trainers who attended the same workshop; one worker at the workshop venue; and 4 hotel staff. They have since been assessed by NCID and offered vaccination to prevent the disease or reduce the severity of symptoms, said the ministry.\n\n\"As a precautionary measure, they will be quarantined and monitored for 21 days from their date of exposure to the patient,\" said MOH. Those who develop symptoms will be treated at NCID, and all others who came into contact with the man and have a low risk of being infected are being put under active surveillance, and will be contacted twice daily to monitor their health status, the ministry added. MOH's investigation and contact tracing operations are ongoing.\n\nCommon symptoms of the disease are fever, headache, muscle ache, backache, swollen lymph nodes and skin rash. In its statement, the MOH said monkeypox is usually self-limiting, with most patients recovering within 2 to 3 weeks.\n\n\"In some cases, however, the virus can cause serious complications such as pneumonia, sepsis, encephalitis (brain inflammation) and eye infection with ensuing loss of vision,\" added the statement. \"There have been reported mortality rates of 1% to 10% during outbreaks, with most deaths occurring in younger age-groups.\"\n\nTransmission of monkeypox mainly occurs when a person comes into close contact with infected animals, usually rodents, through the hunting and consumption of bush meat. It added that transmission of the disease between humans is possible but limited, as a person is infectious only when he has symptoms, particularly skin rash.\n\n\"Transmission typically occurs from close contact with the respiratory tract secretions or skin lesions of an infected person, or objects recently contaminated by an infected person's fluids or lesion materials,\" it added.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This is not the 1st time that a human monkeypox (MPX) virus infection has been imported into another country from Nigeria. In 2018, 2 cases of MPX virus infection were imported into the UK with an additional case ensuing from local transmission from the 2nd patient. The 1st monkeypox case involved a male Nigerian naval officer who was attending a training course at a naval base in Cornwall in southwestern England. The 2nd patient was a UK man who returned to the country from a 22-day vacation in Nigeria on 4 Sep 2018. A 3rd imported case was an Israeli who lived and worked in the Port Harcourt area of Rivers state in southern Nigeria (an endemic MPX region). The man got sick about a week after his return to Israel from Nigeria.\n\nMPX virus is endemic in Nigeria and has been actively transmitted this year (2019). Since the beginning of the outbreak in September 2017, 311 suspected cases and 7 deaths have been reported in 26 states in Nigeria. MPX virus transmission is currently occurring over a broad geographic area in Nigeria. This outbreak has been unusual. Rather than sporadic or rare cases, there have been over 100 cases scattered over a large geographic area since 2017 and again this year (2019). The reasons for this relatively sudden appearance are not clear. Perhaps there has been an epizootic of MPX virus infections among its rodent hosts, with spillover to people.\n\nMonkeypox is an orthopoxvirus infection and is related to smallpox. The disease can also be confused with chickenpox, which is caused by an unrelated virus.\n\nNon-human primates are not monkeypox virus reservoirs. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp., an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents (genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMap of Nigeria: http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf. - Africa (06): Cameroon", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6765533", "date": "2019-05-11 12:00:16", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - SINGAPORE ex NIGERIA\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 9 May 2019\nSource: Straits Times [edited]\nhttps://www.straitstimes.com/singapore/moh-confirms-one-imported-case-of-monkeypox-in-singapore\n\n\nThe Ministry of Health (MOH) has confirmed one imported case of monkeypox infection in Singapore, involving a Nigerian national who arrived last month [April 2019] for a workshop. The 38-year-old man tested positive for the rare viral disease, which is primarily transmitted to humans from animals, on Wednesday (8 May [2019]), said the MOH on Thursday [9 May 2019]. It is the 1st case of monkeypox reported here.\n\nHe is currently in an isolation ward at the National Centre for Infectious Diseases (NCID), and is in stable condition.\n\nMeanwhile, 22 of 23 individuals who have been identified as close contacts of the patient are also under quarantine as a precautionary measure. One of the close contacts, a workshop participant, had left Singapore on 5 May [2019] before the patient was diagnosed. He has reported to the MOH that he remains well with no symptoms.\n\n\"Nonetheless, as a precaution, MOH has informed the public health authority in his home country,\" said the ministry. The MOH added that while it is possible for the disease to be transmitted between humans, the risk of spread is low.\n\nThe MOH offers the following precautions to take when travelling to areas affected by monkeypox in central and western Africa:\n- Maintain a high standard of personal hygiene, including frequent hand washing after going to the toilet, or when hands are soiled.\n- Avoid direct contact with skin lesions of infected living or dead persons or animals, as well as objects that may have become contaminated with infectious fluids, such as soiled clothing or linens (e.g., bedding or towels) used by an infected person.\n- Avoid contact with wild animals, and consumption of bush meat.\n- Returning travellers from areas affected by monkeypox should seek immediate medical attention if they develop any disease symptoms (e.g., sudden onset of high fever, swollen lymph nodes and rash) within 3 weeks of their return. They should inform their doctor of their recent travel history.\n\nBefore coming to Singapore, the man attended a wedding in Nigeria, where he may have consumed bush meat -- a possible source of transmission of the monkeypox virus. Bush meat, the meat of wild animals hunted and sold for food, is a popular source of protein in some parts of Africa where meat from domesticated animals is scarce or expensive.\n\nUpon his arrival on 28 Apr [2019], he stayed at Hotel 81 Orchid in Geylang. In the following 2 days, he attended a workshop at the Samsung Hub in Church Street, in the Central Business District.\n\nOn 30 Apr [2019], he developed fever, muscle ache, chills and skin rash. Between 1 and 7 May [2019], the man reported that he stayed in his hotel room most of the time. On 7 May [2019], he was taken to Tan Tock Seng Hospital by ambulance and referred to NCID.\n\nSubsequently, the MOH identified the people who had come into close contact with the man, including 18 participants and trainers who attended the same workshop; one worker at the workshop venue; and 4 hotel staff. They have since been assessed by NCID and offered vaccination to prevent the disease or reduce the severity of symptoms, said the ministry.\n\n\"As a precautionary measure, they will be quarantined and monitored for 21 days from their date of exposure to the patient,\" said MOH. Those who develop symptoms will be treated at NCID, and all others who came into contact with the man and have a low risk of being infected are being put under active surveillance, and will be contacted twice daily to monitor their health status, the ministry added. MOH's investigation and contact tracing operations are ongoing.\n\nCommon symptoms of the disease are fever, headache, muscle ache, backache, swollen lymph nodes and skin rash. In its statement, the MOH said monkeypox is usually self-limiting, with most patients recovering within 2 to 3 weeks.\n\n\"In some cases, however, the virus can cause serious complications such as pneumonia, sepsis, encephalitis (brain inflammation) and eye infection with ensuing loss of vision,\" added the statement. \"There have been reported mortality rates of 1% to 10% during outbreaks, with most deaths occurring in younger age-groups.\"\n\nTransmission of monkeypox mainly occurs when a person comes into close contact with infected animals, usually rodents, through the hunting and consumption of bush meat. It added that transmission of the disease between humans is possible but limited, as a person is infectious only when he has symptoms, particularly skin rash.\n\n\"Transmission typically occurs from close contact with the respiratory tract secretions or skin lesions of an infected person, or objects recently contaminated by an infected person's fluids or lesion materials,\" it added.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This is not the 1st time that a human monkeypox (MPX) virus infection has been imported into another country from Nigeria. In 2018, 2 cases of MPX virus infection were imported into the UK with an additional case ensuing from local transmission from the 2nd patient. The 1st monkeypox case involved a male Nigerian naval officer who was attending a training course at a naval base in Cornwall in southwestern England. The 2nd patient was a UK man who returned to the country from a 22-day vacation in Nigeria on 4 Sep 2018. A 3rd imported case was an Israeli who lived and worked in the Port Harcourt area of Rivers state in southern Nigeria (an endemic MPX region). The man got sick about a week after his return to Israel from Nigeria.\n\nMPX virus is endemic in Nigeria and has been actively transmitted this year (2019). Since the beginning of the outbreak in September 2017, 311 suspected cases and 7 deaths have been reported in 26 states in Nigeria. MPX virus transmission is currently occurring over a broad geographic area in Nigeria. This outbreak has been unusual. Rather than sporadic or rare cases, there have been over 100 cases scattered over a large geographic area since 2017 and again this year (2019). The reasons for this relatively sudden appearance are not clear. Perhaps there has been an epizootic of MPX virus infections among its rodent hosts, with spillover to people.\n\nMonkeypox is an orthopoxvirus infection and is related to smallpox. The disease can also be confused with chickenpox, which is caused by an unrelated virus.\n\nNon-human primates are not monkeypox virus reservoirs. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp., an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents (genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMap of Nigeria: http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf. - They should inform their doctor of their recent travel history.\n\nBefore coming to Singapore, the man attended a wedding in Nigeria, where he may have consumed bush meat -- a possible source of transmission of the monkeypox virus. Bush meat, the meat of wild animals hunted and sold for food, is a popular source of protein in some parts of Africa where meat from domesticated animals is scarce or expensive.\n\nUpon his arrival on 28 Apr [2019], he stayed at Hotel 81 Orchid in Geylang. In the following 2 days, he attended a workshop at the Samsung Hub in Church Street, in the Central Business District.\n\nOn 30 Apr [2019], he developed fever, muscle ache, chills and skin rash. Between 1 and 7 May [2019], the man reported that he stayed in his hotel room most of the time. On 7 May [2019], he was taken to Tan Tock Seng Hospital by ambulance and referred to NCID.\n\nSubsequently, the MOH identified the people who had come into close contact with the man, including 18 participants and trainers who attended the same workshop; one worker at the workshop venue; and 4 hotel staff. They have since been assessed by NCID and offered vaccination to prevent the disease or reduce the severity of symptoms, said the ministry.\n\n\"As a precautionary measure, they will be quarantined and monitored for 21 days from their date of exposure to the patient,\" said MOH. Those who develop symptoms will be treated at NCID, and all others who came into contact with the man and have a low risk of being infected are being put under active surveillance, and will be contacted twice daily to monitor their health status, the ministry added. MOH's investigation and contact tracing operations are ongoing.\n\nCommon symptoms of the disease are fever, headache, muscle ache, backache, swollen lymph nodes and skin rash. In its statement, the MOH said monkeypox is usually self-limiting, with most patients recovering within 2 to 3 weeks.\n\n\"In some cases, however, the virus can cause serious complications such as pneumonia, sepsis, encephalitis (brain inflammation) and eye infection with ensuing loss of vision,\" added the statement. \"There have been reported mortality rates of 1% to 10% during outbreaks, with most deaths occurring in younger age-groups.\"\n\nTransmission of monkeypox mainly occurs when a person comes into close contact with infected animals, usually rodents, through the hunting and consumption of bush meat. It added that transmission of the disease between humans is possible but limited, as a person is infectious only when he has symptoms, particularly skin rash.\n\n\"Transmission typically occurs from close contact with the respiratory tract secretions or skin lesions of an infected person, or objects recently contaminated by an infected person's fluids or lesion materials,\" it added.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This is not the 1st time that a human monkeypox (MPX) virus infection has been imported into another country from Nigeria. In 2018, 2 cases of MPX virus infection were imported into the UK with an additional case ensuing from local transmission from the 2nd patient. The 1st monkeypox case involved a male Nigerian naval officer who was attending a training course at a naval base in Cornwall in southwestern England. The 2nd patient was a UK man who returned to the country from a 22-day vacation in Nigeria on 4 Sep 2018. A 3rd imported case was an Israeli who lived and worked in the Port Harcourt area of Rivers state in southern Nigeria (an endemic MPX region). The man got sick about a week after his return to Israel from Nigeria.\n\nMPX virus is endemic in Nigeria and has been actively transmitted this year (2019). Since the beginning of the outbreak in September 2017, 311 suspected cases and 7 deaths have been reported in 26 states in Nigeria. MPX virus transmission is currently occurring over a broad geographic area in Nigeria. This outbreak has been unusual. Rather than sporadic or rare cases, there have been over 100 cases scattered over a large geographic area since 2017 and again this year (2019). The reasons for this relatively sudden appearance are not clear. Perhaps there has been an epizootic of MPX virus infections among its rodent hosts, with spillover to people.\n\nMonkeypox is an orthopoxvirus infection and is related to smallpox. The disease can also be confused with chickenpox, which is caused by an unrelated virus.\n\nNon-human primates are not monkeypox virus reservoirs. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp., an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents (genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMap of Nigeria: http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf. - The 38-year-old man tested positive for the rare viral disease, which is primarily transmitted to humans from animals, on Wednesday (8 May [2019]), said the MOH on Thursday [9 May 2019]. It is the 1st case of monkeypox reported here.\n\nHe is currently in an isolation ward at the National Centre for Infectious Diseases (NCID), and is in stable condition.\n\nMeanwhile, 22 of 23 individuals who have been identified as close contacts of the patient are also under quarantine as a precautionary measure. One of the close contacts, a workshop participant, had left Singapore on 5 May [2019] before the patient was diagnosed. He has reported to the MOH that he remains well with no symptoms.\n\n\"Nonetheless, as a precaution, MOH has informed the public health authority in his home country,\" said the ministry. The MOH added that while it is possible for the disease to be transmitted between humans, the risk of spread is low.\n\nThe MOH offers the following precautions to take when travelling to areas affected by monkeypox in central and western Africa:\n- Maintain a high standard of personal hygiene, including frequent hand washing after going to the toilet, or when hands are soiled.\n- Avoid direct contact with skin lesions of infected living or dead persons or animals, as well as objects that may have become contaminated with infectious fluids, such as soiled clothing or linens (e.g., bedding or towels) used by an infected person.\n- Avoid contact with wild animals, and consumption of bush meat.\n- Returning travellers from areas affected by monkeypox should seek immediate medical attention if they develop any disease symptoms (e.g., sudden onset of high fever, swollen lymph nodes and rash) within 3 weeks of their return. They should inform their doctor of their recent travel history.\n\nBefore coming to Singapore, the man attended a wedding in Nigeria, where he may have consumed bush meat -- a possible source of transmission of the monkeypox virus. Bush meat, the meat of wild animals hunted and sold for food, is a popular source of protein in some parts of Africa where meat from domesticated animals is scarce or expensive.\n\nUpon his arrival on 28 Apr [2019], he stayed at Hotel 81 Orchid in Geylang. This 2nd patient had travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have acquired the infection. The 2nd case 1st presented at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and following a positive test result was transferred to Royal Liverpool University Hospital, an expert respiratory infectious disease centre, where they are receiving appropriate care.\n\nDr Nick Phin, Deputy Director, National Infection Service at PHE:\n\nWe know that in September 2017, Nigeria experienced a large sustained outbreak of monkeypox, and, since then, sporadic cases have continued to be reported. It is likely that monkeypox continues to circulate in Nigeria and could, therefore, affect travellers who are returning from this part of the world.\n\nHowever, it is very unusual to see 2 cases in such a relatively short space of time. We are working hard to contact individuals, including healthcare workers, that might have come into contact with the individual to provide information and health advice.\n\nDr Mike Beadsworth, Clinical Director of the Tropical and Infectious Diseases Unit, said, \"We are treating a patient who has tested positive for monkeypox. The patient is being cared for on our specialist infectious and tropical diseases unit by highly trained staff who are experienced in dealing with a variety of infectious diseases. All necessary precautions are being taken by specialist staff, and there is currently no risk to other staff, patients or visitors. We ask that people continue to use our services as normal and that people only come to our emergency department if their condition is serious and/or an emergency.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2] BBC report\nDate: Tue 11 Sept 2018\nSource: BBC [edited]\nhttps://www.bbc.com/news/uk-england-lancashire-45490444\n\n\nA 2nd case of monkeypox has been identified in the UK, just days after the 1st was discovered.\n\nThe patient tested positive at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and had recently travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have contracted it, Public Health England said. They have been transferred to Liverpool University Hospital for treatment.\n\nThe 1st UK case was diagnosed in Cornwall, in a patient who had also spent time in Nigeria.\n\nThe rare viral infection does not spread easily between humans, and most people recover within a few weeks.\n\nPHE said there was no UK link between the [2] patients.\n\nWhat is monkeypox?\n- Monkeypox is usually a mild self-limiting illness, and most people recover within a few weeks; however, severe illness can occur in some.\n- It is a rare disease caused by monkeypox virus, and has been reported mainly in central and west African countries.\n- It can spread when someone is in close contact with an infected person; however, there is a very low risk of transmission to the general population.\n- Initial symptoms include fever, headache, muscle aches, backache, swollen lymph nodes, chills and exhaustion.\n- A rash can develop, often beginning on the face, then spreading to other parts of the body. The rash changes and goes through different stages before finally forming a scab, which later falls off. UK (05): ex Nigeria", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6069282", "date": "2018-09-11 20:36:21", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - UK (02): ex NIGERIA\n*******************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nIn this report:\n[1] PHE press release\n[2] BBC report\n[3] Cumbria Crack report\n\n******\n[1] PHE press release\nDate: Tue 11 Sep 2018\nSource: Public Health England [edited]\nhttps://www.gov.uk/government/news/monkeypox-case-in-england\n\n\nPublic Health England (PHE) can confirm that a 2nd individual has been diagnosed with monkeypox in England.\n\nThere is no UK link to the patient diagnosed last week in Cornwall who is currently receiving care at the Royal Free in London. This 2nd patient had travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have acquired the infection. The 2nd case 1st presented at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and following a positive test result was transferred to Royal Liverpool University Hospital, an expert respiratory infectious disease centre, where they are receiving appropriate care.\n\nDr Nick Phin, Deputy Director, National Infection Service at PHE:\n\nWe know that in September 2017, Nigeria experienced a large sustained outbreak of monkeypox, and, since then, sporadic cases have continued to be reported. It is likely that monkeypox continues to circulate in Nigeria and could, therefore, affect travellers who are returning from this part of the world.\n\nHowever, it is very unusual to see 2 cases in such a relatively short space of time. We are working hard to contact individuals, including healthcare workers, that might have come into contact with the individual to provide information and health advice.\n\nDr Mike Beadsworth, Clinical Director of the Tropical and Infectious Diseases Unit, said, \"We are treating a patient who has tested positive for monkeypox. The patient is being cared for on our specialist infectious and tropical diseases unit by highly trained staff who are experienced in dealing with a variety of infectious diseases. All necessary precautions are being taken by specialist staff, and there is currently no risk to other staff, patients or visitors. We ask that people continue to use our services as normal and that people only come to our emergency department if their condition is serious and/or an emergency.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2] BBC report\nDate: Tue 11 Sept 2018\nSource: BBC [edited]\nhttps://www.bbc.com/news/uk-england-lancashire-45490444\n\n\nA 2nd case of monkeypox has been identified in the UK, just days after the 1st was discovered.\n\nThe patient tested positive at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and had recently travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have contracted it, Public Health England said. They have been transferred to Liverpool University Hospital for treatment.\n\nThe 1st UK case was diagnosed in Cornwall, in a patient who had also spent time in Nigeria.\n\nThe rare viral infection does not spread easily between humans, and most people recover within a few weeks.\n\nPHE said there was no UK link between the [2] patients.\n\nWhat is monkeypox?\n- Monkeypox is usually a mild self-limiting illness, and most people recover within a few weeks; however, severe illness can occur in some.\n- It is a rare disease caused by monkeypox virus, and has been reported mainly in central and west African countries.\n- It can spread when someone is in close contact with an infected person; however, there is a very low risk of transmission to the general population.\n- Initial symptoms include fever, headache, muscle aches, backache, swollen lymph nodes, chills and exhaustion.\n- A rash can develop, often beginning on the face, then spreading to other parts of the body. The rash changes and goes through different stages before finally forming a scab, which later falls off. [Source: Public Health England]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[3] Cumbria Crack report\nDate: Tue 11 Sep 2018\nSource: Cumbria Crack [edited]\nhttps://www.cumbriacrack.com/2018/09/11/case-of-monkeypox-confirmed-in-blackpool/\n\n\nPublic Health England (PHE) can confirm that a 2nd individual has been diagnosed with monkeypox in Blackpool.\n\nThere is no UK link to the patient diagnosed last week in Cornwall who is currently receiving care at the Royal Free in London. This 2nd patient had travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have acquired the infection. The 2nd case 1st presented at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and following a positive test result was transferred to Royal Liverpool University Hospital, an expert respiratory infectious disease centre, where they are receiving appropriate care.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[It is interesting that the UK had its 1st ever monkeypox case diagnosed 3 days ago and now has a 2nd case. No indication is given about where in Nigeria these 2 cases might have acquired their infections. There are several possibilities. Nigeria has had many monkeypox cases in 2017-2018 in several states. The most recent cases were in June 2018 in Plateau state. These cases in Nigeria were caused by the West African clade of the monkeypox virus, presumably the same clade of the virus as infects the 2 UK cases.\n\nNon-human primates are not a monkeypox virus reservoir. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp; an arboreal rodent, and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf. - Africa (13): Nigeria", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6026151", "date": "2018-09-11 20:36:21", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - UK (02): ex NIGERIA\n*******************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nIn this report:\n[1] PHE press release\n[2] BBC report\n[3] Cumbria Crack report\n\n******\n[1] PHE press release\nDate: Tue 11 Sep 2018\nSource: Public Health England [edited]\nhttps://www.gov.uk/government/news/monkeypox-case-in-england\n\n\nPublic Health England (PHE) can confirm that a 2nd individual has been diagnosed with monkeypox in England.\n\nThere is no UK link to the patient diagnosed last week in Cornwall who is currently receiving care at the Royal Free in London. This 2nd patient had travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have acquired the infection. The 2nd case 1st presented at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and following a positive test result was transferred to Royal Liverpool University Hospital, an expert respiratory infectious disease centre, where they are receiving appropriate care.\n\nDr Nick Phin, Deputy Director, National Infection Service at PHE:\n\nWe know that in September 2017, Nigeria experienced a large sustained outbreak of monkeypox, and, since then, sporadic cases have continued to be reported. It is likely that monkeypox continues to circulate in Nigeria and could, therefore, affect travellers who are returning from this part of the world.\n\nHowever, it is very unusual to see 2 cases in such a relatively short space of time. We are working hard to contact individuals, including healthcare workers, that might have come into contact with the individual to provide information and health advice.\n\nDr Mike Beadsworth, Clinical Director of the Tropical and Infectious Diseases Unit, said, \"We are treating a patient who has tested positive for monkeypox. The patient is being cared for on our specialist infectious and tropical diseases unit by highly trained staff who are experienced in dealing with a variety of infectious diseases. All necessary precautions are being taken by specialist staff, and there is currently no risk to other staff, patients or visitors. We ask that people continue to use our services as normal and that people only come to our emergency department if their condition is serious and/or an emergency.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2] BBC report\nDate: Tue 11 Sept 2018\nSource: BBC [edited]\nhttps://www.bbc.com/news/uk-england-lancashire-45490444\n\n\nA 2nd case of monkeypox has been identified in the UK, just days after the 1st was discovered.\n\nThe patient tested positive at Blackpool Victoria Hospital and had recently travelled to Nigeria where they are believed to have contracted it, Public Health England said. The rash changes and goes through different stages before finally forming a scab, which later falls off. It is likely that monkeypox continues to circulate in Nigeria and could, therefore, affect travellers who are returning from this part of the world.\n\nHowever, it is very unusual to see 2 cases in such a relatively short space of time. We are working hard to contact individuals, including healthcare workers, that might have come into contact with the individual to provide information and health advice.\n\nDr Mike Beadsworth, Clinical Director of the Tropical and Infectious Diseases Unit, said, \"We are treating a patient who has tested positive for monkeypox. Reemergence of human monkeypox in Nigeria, 2017. Emerg Infect Dis. 2018 Jun; 24 (6) [date cited]. https://doi.org/10.3201/eid2406.180017\n\nAbstract\n--------\nIn Nigeria, before 2017 the most recent case of human monkeypox had been reported in 1978. By mid-November 2017, a large outbreak caused by the West African clade resulted in 146 suspected cases and 42 laboratory-confirmed cases from 14 states. Although the source is unknown, multiple sources are suspected.\n\n[Selected summary]\n------------------\nOn [13 Oct 2017], the Nigeria CDC received laboratory confirmation of a human monkeypox outbreak in Nigeria. As of [17 Nov 2017], a total of 146 suspected cases had been reported from 22 of the 36 states in Nigeria (Figure [see URL]). Of the 134 samples (blood, lesion swab, and crust) collected during the reporting period, 107 samples were tested, and 42 samples from 14 states were laboratory confirmed as the West African clade of the monkeypox virus. Most (62 percent) of the laboratory-confirmed cases were in adults (21-40 years of age; median 30 years of age); the male:female ratio was 2:1. A 46-year-old male patient with confirmed monkeypox and a history of immunosuppressive illness died. For some patients with suspected (but ultimately deemed negative) cases of monkeypox, chickenpox (wild-type virus) was confirmed. Further analysis of the monkeypox-negative samples is ongoing.\n\nAlthough detailed epidemiologic investigations to ascertain the source and route of transmission are ongoing, 3 family clusters were found, which might suggest some level of human-to-human transmission in this outbreak. For 1 of the families, the secondary attack rate was 71 percent. However, most patients had no obvious epidemiologic linkage or person-to-person contact, indicating a probable multiple-source outbreak or possibly previously unrecognized endemic disease. The zoonotic source(s) of the outbreak are currently unknown, and it is unclear what, if any, environmental or ecologic changes might have facilitated its sudden reemergence.\n\nDisclaimer [from the URL]: Ahead of print articles are not considered as final versions. Any changes will be reflected in the online version in the month the article is officially released.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This report provides a good summary of the recent monkeypox (MPX) outbreak in Nigeria. It substantiates the West African clade of MPX virus as the one involved, which explains the low (almost nil) case fatality rate compared to the Central African clade. The 3 family clusters indicate that person-to-person transmission can occur, but does not account for the origin of the majority of cases. The role of rodents as reservoirs and sources of human exposure in this outbreak are unknown.\n\nA previous report indicated that the MPXV of the index case was acquired within the country. With no cross-state relationships of cases and preliminary genetic sequencing, that suggests multiple sources of introduction of monkeypox virus into human population. One wonders if a MPXV epizootic has been going on in rodent hosts across a relatively wide geographic area in the country. Studies of prevalence of MPXV in rodent hosts are not mentioned in this or in previous reports. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/62. - Africa (10): Cameroon", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5838919", "date": "2018-04-05 15:29:28", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (06): NIGERIA\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate Wed 4 Apr 2018 [ahead of print]\nSource: Emerging Infectious Diseases journal [summ., edited]\nhttps://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/24/6/18-0017_article#suggestedcitation\n\n\nCitation\n-------\nYinka-Ogunleye A, Aruna O, Ogoina D, et al. Reemergence of human monkeypox in Nigeria, 2017. Emerg Infect Dis. 2018 Jun; 24 (6) [date cited]. https://doi.org/10.3201/eid2406.180017\n\nAbstract\n--------\nIn Nigeria, before 2017 the most recent case of human monkeypox had been reported in 1978. By mid-November 2017, a large outbreak caused by the West African clade resulted in 146 suspected cases and 42 laboratory-confirmed cases from 14 states. Although the source is unknown, multiple sources are suspected.\n\n[Selected summary]\n------------------\nOn [13 Oct 2017], the Nigeria CDC received laboratory confirmation of a human monkeypox outbreak in Nigeria. As of [17 Nov 2017], a total of 146 suspected cases had been reported from 22 of the 36 states in Nigeria (Figure [see URL]). Of the 134 samples (blood, lesion swab, and crust) collected during the reporting period, 107 samples were tested, and 42 samples from 14 states were laboratory confirmed as the West African clade of the monkeypox virus. Most (62 percent) of the laboratory-confirmed cases were in adults (21-40 years of age; median 30 years of age); the male:female ratio was 2:1. A 46-year-old male patient with confirmed monkeypox and a history of immunosuppressive illness died. For some patients with suspected (but ultimately deemed negative) cases of monkeypox, chickenpox (wild-type virus) was confirmed. Further analysis of the monkeypox-negative samples is ongoing.\n\nAlthough detailed epidemiologic investigations to ascertain the source and route of transmission are ongoing, 3 family clusters were found, which might suggest some level of human-to-human transmission in this outbreak. For 1 of the families, the secondary attack rate was 71 percent. However, most patients had no obvious epidemiologic linkage or person-to-person contact, indicating a probable multiple-source outbreak or possibly previously unrecognized endemic disease. The zoonotic source(s) of the outbreak are currently unknown, and it is unclear what, if any, environmental or ecologic changes might have facilitated its sudden reemergence.\n\nDisclaimer [from the URL]: Ahead of print articles are not considered as final versions. Any changes will be reflected in the online version in the month the article is officially released.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This report provides a good summary of the recent monkeypox (MPX) outbreak in Nigeria. It substantiates the West African clade of MPX virus as the one involved, which explains the low (almost nil) case fatality rate compared to the Central African clade. The 3 family clusters indicate that person-to-person transmission can occur, but does not account for the origin of the majority of cases. The role of rodents as reservoirs and sources of human exposure in this outbreak are unknown.\n\nA previous report indicated that the MPXV of the index case was acquired within the country. With no cross-state relationships of cases and preliminary genetic sequencing, that suggests multiple sources of introduction of monkeypox virus into human population. One wonders if a MPXV epizootic has been going on in rodent hosts across a relatively wide geographic area in the country. Studies of prevalence of MPXV in rodent hosts are not mentioned in this or in previous reports. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/62. - Emerg Infect Dis. 2018 Jun; 24 (6) [date cited]. https://doi.org/10.3201/eid2406.180017\n\nAbstract\n--------\nIn Nigeria, before 2017 the most recent case of human monkeypox had been reported in 1978. By mid-November 2017, a large outbreak caused by the West African clade resulted in 146 suspected cases and 42 laboratory-confirmed cases from 14 states. Although the source is unknown, multiple sources are suspected.\n\n[Selected summary]\n------------------\nOn [13 Oct 2017], the Nigeria CDC received laboratory confirmation of a human monkeypox outbreak in Nigeria. As of [17 Nov 2017], a total of 146 suspected cases had been reported from 22 of the 36 states in Nigeria (Figure [see URL]). Of the 134 samples (blood, lesion swab, and crust) collected during the reporting period, 107 samples were tested, and 42 samples from 14 states were laboratory confirmed as the West African clade of the monkeypox virus. Most (62 percent) of the laboratory-confirmed cases were in adults (21-40 years of age; median 30 years of age); the male:female ratio was 2:1. A 46-year-old male patient with confirmed monkeypox and a history of immunosuppressive illness died. For some patients with suspected (but ultimately deemed negative) cases of monkeypox, chickenpox (wild-type virus) was confirmed. Further analysis of the monkeypox-negative samples is ongoing.\n\nAlthough detailed epidemiologic investigations to ascertain the source and route of transmission are ongoing, 3 family clusters were found, which might suggest some level of human-to-human transmission in this outbreak. For 1 of the families, the secondary attack rate was 71 percent. However, most patients had no obvious epidemiologic linkage or person-to-person contact, indicating a probable multiple-source outbreak or possibly previously unrecognized endemic disease. The zoonotic source(s) of the outbreak are currently unknown, and it is unclear what, if any, environmental or ecologic changes might have facilitated its sudden reemergence.\n\nDisclaimer [from the URL]: Ahead of print articles are not considered as final versions. Any changes will be reflected in the online version in the month the article is officially released.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This report provides a good summary of the recent monkeypox (MPX) outbreak in Nigeria. It substantiates the West African clade of MPX virus as the one involved, which explains the low (almost nil) case fatality rate compared to the Central African clade. The 3 family clusters indicate that person-to-person transmission can occur, but does not account for the origin of the majority of cases. The role of rodents as reservoirs and sources of human exposure in this outbreak are unknown.\n\nA previous report indicated that the MPXV of the index case was acquired within the country. With no cross-state relationships of cases and preliminary genetic sequencing, that suggests multiple sources of introduction of monkeypox virus into human population. One wonders if a MPXV epizootic has been going on in rodent hosts across a relatively wide geographic area in the country. Studies of prevalence of MPXV in rodent hosts are not mentioned in this or in previous reports. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/62. - Mod.TY\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nNigeria: "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa: Nigeria", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5572204", "date": "2017-11-22 03:28:33", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (18): NIGERIA\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Sun 19 Nov 2017\nSource: Nigeria Center for Disease Control: Monkeypox outbreak in Nigeria situation report (No. 008) [edited]\nhttp://www.ncdc.gov.ng/themes/common/files/sitreps/72cb569d609098f0998f92d253c85e58.pdf\n\n\nHighlights\n- The Nigeria Centre for Disease Control (NCDC) continued to coordinate the response through its activated Emergency Operations Centre (EOC)\n- 4 new positive results were recorded from previously reported cases in Cross River (2), Imo (1) and Delta (1) states\n- 14 new suspected cases reported in the last one week\n- NCDC National Reference Laboratory, Abuja continued laboratory diagnosis of monkeypox\n- There has been a decline in the number of new suspected cases reported over the last 3 weeks\n- Since the onset of the outbreak, a total of 146 cases have been recorded from 21 states and the Federal Capital Territory (FCT)\n- No death attributable to monkeypox has been recorded so far\n- Laboratory investigation has so far confirmed 42 cases of monkeypox in 10 states (Akwa, Ibom, Bayelsa, Cross River, Delta, Edo, Ekiti, Enugu, Imo, Lagos, Rivers) and the FCT\n- Suspected cases reported from other states are being investigated\n- In furtherance of the one health approach to the outbreak response, a multi-sectoral team of human and animal epidemiologists have been deployed to some affected states to conduct detailed outbreak investigations among human and animal populations\n\nEpidemiological summary\n- 42 laboratory confirmed cases recorded since the onset of the outbreak\n- A total of 146 suspected cases have been reported from 21 states (Abia, Akwa-Ibom, Bayelsa, Benue, Cross River, Delta, Ekiti, Edo, Enugu, Imo, Kaduna, Kano, Katsina, Kwara, Kogi, Lagos, Ondo, Nasarawa, Niger, Oyo, Rivers) and the FCT\n- 9 of 12 monkeypox negative swab samples tested positive for chickenpox\n- A total of 134 samples have been collected and sent for testing\n- The male to female ratio is 2:1\n- The most affected age group is 21-40 years (median age = 30)\n- No deaths recorded among cases so far\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[The number of confirmed monkeypox (MPX) cases continues to increase, although more slowly, from 38 cases in 8 states on 7 Nov 2017 to 42 cases in 10 states currently. The MPX virus clade involved in this outbreak is not mentioned. With no reported fatalities, the West Africa clade may be the one involved in this outbreak. Interestingly, some of the cases reported above negative for MPX tested positive for chickenpox, so there appears to be a mixed outbreak involving these 2 unrelated but clinically similar viruses. As noted in the previous post, this is an unusually widespread outbreak of the disease. It would be of interest to know whether there is evidence of a monkeypox outbreak in these states in rodents that are the suspected reservoirs. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/62. - Mod.TY]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (17): "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa (20): Nigeria", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5516079", "date": "2017-11-22 03:28:33", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (18): NIGERIA\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Sun 19 Nov 2017\nSource: Nigeria Center for Disease Control: Monkeypox outbreak in Nigeria situation report (No. 008) [edited]\nhttp://www.ncdc.gov.ng/themes/common/files/sitreps/72cb569d609098f0998f92d253c85e58.pdf\n\n\nHighlights\n- The Nigeria Centre for Disease Control (NCDC) continued to coordinate the response through its activated Emergency Operations Centre (EOC)\n- 4 new positive results were recorded from previously reported cases in Cross River (2), Imo (1) and Delta (1) states\n- 14 new suspected cases reported in the last one week\n- NCDC National Reference Laboratory, Abuja continued laboratory diagnosis of monkeypox\n- There has been a decline in the number of new suspected cases reported over the last 3 weeks\n- Since the onset of the outbreak, a total of 146 cases have been recorded from 21 states and the Federal Capital Territory (FCT)\n- No death attributable to monkeypox has been recorded so far\n- Laboratory investigation has so far confirmed 42 cases of monkeypox in 10 states (Akwa, Ibom, Bayelsa, Cross River, Delta, Edo, Ekiti, Enugu, Imo, Lagos, Rivers) and the FCT\n- Suspected cases reported from other states are being investigated\n- In furtherance of the one health approach to the outbreak response, a multi-sectoral team of human and animal epidemiologists have been deployed to some affected states to conduct detailed outbreak investigations among human and animal populations\n\nEpidemiological summary\n- 42 laboratory confirmed cases recorded since the onset of the outbreak\n- A total of 146 suspected cases have been reported from 21 states (Abia, Akwa-Ibom, Bayelsa, Benue, Cross River, Delta, Ekiti, Edo, Enugu, Imo, Kaduna, Kano, Katsina, Kwara, Kogi, Lagos, Ondo, Nasarawa, Niger, Oyo, Rivers) and the FCT\n- 9 of 12 monkeypox negative swab samples tested positive for chickenpox\n- A total of 134 samples have been collected and sent for testing\n- The male to female ratio is 2:1\n- The most affected age group is 21-40 years (median age = 30)\n- No deaths recorded among cases so far\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[The number of confirmed monkeypox (MPX) cases continues to increase, although more slowly, from 38 cases in 8 states on 7 Nov 2017 to 42 cases in 10 states currently. The MPX virus clade involved in this outbreak is not mentioned. With no reported fatalities, the West Africa clade may be the one involved in this outbreak. Interestingly, some of the cases reported above negative for MPX tested positive for chickenpox, so there appears to be a mixed outbreak involving these 2 unrelated but clinically similar viruses. As noted in the previous post, this is an unusually widespread outbreak of the disease. It would be of interest to know whether there is evidence of a monkeypox outbreak in these states in rodents that are the suspected reservoirs. The main reservoirs of monkeypox virus are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ spp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/62. - Mod.TY]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (17): onset of the outbreak\n- A total of 146 suspected cases have been reported from 21 states (Abia, Akwa-Ibom, Bayelsa, Benue, Cross River, Delta, Ekiti, Edo, Enugu, Imo, Kaduna, Kano, Katsina, Kwara, Kogi, Lagos, Ondo, Nasarawa, Niger, Oyo, Rivers) and the FCT\n- 9 of 12 monkeypox negative swab samples tested positive for chickenpox\n- A total of 134 samples have been collected and sent for Marshall\n\n[The number of confirmed monkeypox (MPX) cases continues to increase, although more slowly, from 38 cases in 8 states on 7 Nov 2017 to 42 cases in 10 states currently. The MPX virus clade involved in this outbreak is not mentioned. With no reported fatalities, the West Africa clade may be the one involved in this outbreak. Interestingly, some of the cases reported above negative for MPX tested positive for chickenpox, so there appears to be a mixed outbreak involving these 2 unrelated but clinically similar viruses. As noted in the previous post, this is an unusually widespread outbreak of the disease. It would be of interest to know whether there is evidence of a monkeypox outbreak in these states in rodents that are the suspected reservoirs. These 3 samples are positive for monkeypox. Prof. Tomori suspects that these are from the index case. It is good that these samples are being sent to the USA CDC for further confirmation. The question about the laboratory diagnosis of the other 30 cases in 7 states is still open. ProMED-mail is awaiting the results from laboratory tests from samples taken from these cases with considerable interest. If these are laboratory confirmed as monkeypox, this will be an unusual monkeypox outbreak given the wide geographic area in which the cases occurred during the same period. One health authority in Nigeria has indicated that at least some of these cases are varicella (chickenpox) (see Monkeypox - Africa (09): Nigeria, hospital cases NOT, other states' cases susp. 20171014.5380964).\n\nMaps of Nigeria can be accessed at http://www.un.org/Depts/Cartographic/map/profile/nigeria.pdf and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/62. - Mod.TY]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (09): Nigeria, hospital cases NOT, other states' cases susp. 20171014.5380964\nMonkeypox - Africa (08): Nigeria 20171010.5369514\nMonkeypox - Africa (07): Nigeria 20171009.5368881\nMonkeypox - Africa (06): Nigeria 20171007.5366637\n.................................................lm/ml/ty/msp/ml", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "varicella", "monkeypox"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-09 00:00:00", "2021-03-09 00:00:00", "2021-03-08 00:00:00", "2021-03-07 00:00:00", "2021-03-06 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Nigeria", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "5439201", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox - Africa (16): Nigeria (DE) conf. susp.", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5439201", "date": "2017-10-16 21:17:28", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - AFRICA (10): NIGERIA\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Mon 16 Oct 2017\nFrom: Oyewale Tomori <oyewaletomori@gmail.com> [edited]\n\n\nThe latest from Nigeria is that 3 samples were confirmed positive in the Dakar lab.\n\nI am not sure which samples are positive, but suspect they are from the index case. He presented to a local health facility on [Thu 16 Mar 2017] with fever, body pain, malaise, dysphagia, and enlarged cervical lymph nodes; and was presumptively treated for malaria and sore throat as an out-patient.\n\nOn [Fri 17 Mar 2017], he developed generalized vesicular skin eruptions in addition to the initial constitutional clinical features. The case-patient was admitted to Pujehun district hospital on [Sat 25 Mar 2017].\n\nLaboratory results from Institut National de Recherche Biomedicale (INRB) in Kinshasha, DRC last week [week of 16 April 2017] indicated that the vesicular swab tested positive for Orthopox virus by polymerase chain reaction (PCR) assay and monkeypox virus by GeneXpert technique. Based on these results, the Sierra Leone Ministry of Health notified WHO on [Mon 17 Apr 2017] of the outbreak of monkeypox.\n\nA total of 13 close contacts to the index case were listed and are being followed up. None of them have developed any febrile illness and/or skin lesions in the 1st 21 days since the last exposure. The contacts are still being monitored for additional 21 days (twice the maximum incubation period of monkeypox).\n\nThis is the 2nd sporadic occurrence of monkeypox in the country, with the 1st one reported in March 2014 in Bo district located in the southern region of the country.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Occurrence of sporadic cases is not surprising because monkeypox virus is endemic in West Africa, including Sierra Leone and human cases occur sporadically in the region.\n\nAs noted in previous ProMED-mail posts, the monkeypox virus clade in the Congo Basin causes more severe disease in humans, with a case fatality rate of 11-17 percent, than the clade in West Africa, which causes few fatalities. The virus is seldom transmitted directly from one person to another, but there is a documented instance of that in the Republic of the Congo. Monkeys are not the reservoirs of the virus, despite the name that the virus has received. Although not determined, the main reservoirs of monkeypox are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ sp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_. - Mod.TY\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/46.]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (02): Republic of the Congo 20170404.4947955\nMonkeypox - Africa: Republic of the Congo 20170315.4903754\n2016\n----\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (04): (HK) WHO 20161014.4560870\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (03): (BK,HK) fatal 20160929.4525807\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (02): (BK) fatal 20160924.4512897\nMonkeypox, chimpanzee - Cameroon: (CE) emerging, OIE 20160827.4445967\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (02): (BU) 20160212.4014697\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic: (MB) RFI 20160119.3947756\n2015\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp. He presented to a local health facility on [Thu 16 Mar 2017] with fever, body pain, malaise, dysphagia, and enlarged cervical lymph nodes; and was presumptively treated for malaria and sore throat as an out-patient.\n\nOn [Fri 17 Mar 2017], he developed generalized vesicular skin eruptions in addition to the initial constitutional clinical features. The case-patient was admitted to Pujehun district hospital on [Sat 25 Mar 2017].\n\nLaboratory results from Institut National de Recherche Biomedicale (INRB) in Kinshasha, DRC last week [week of 16 April 2017] indicated that the vesicular swab tested positive for Orthopox virus by polymerase chain reaction (PCR) assay and monkeypox virus by GeneXpert technique. Based on these results, the Sierra Leone Ministry of Health notified WHO on [Mon 17 Apr 2017] of the outbreak of monkeypox.\n\nA total of 13 close contacts to the index case were listed and are being followed up. None of them have developed any febrile illness and/or skin lesions in the 1st 21 days since the last exposure. The contacts are still being monitored for additional 21 days (twice the maximum incubation period of monkeypox).\n\nThis is the 2nd sporadic occurrence of monkeypox in the country, with the 1st one reported in March 2014 in Bo district located in the southern region of the country.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Occurrence of sporadic cases is not surprising because monkeypox virus is endemic in West Africa, including Sierra Leone and human cases occur sporadically in the region.\n\nAs noted in previous ProMED-mail posts, the monkeypox virus clade in the Congo Basin causes more severe disease in humans, with a case fatality rate of 11-17 percent, than the clade in West Africa, which causes few fatalities. The virus is seldom transmitted directly from one person to another, but there is a documented instance of that in the Republic of the Congo. Monkeys are not the reservoirs of the virus, despite the name that the virus has received. Although not determined, the main reservoirs of monkeypox are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ sp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_. - Mod.TY\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/46.]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (02): Republic of the Congo 20170404.4947955\nMonkeypox - Africa: Republic of the Congo 20170315.4903754\n2016\n----\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (04): (HK) WHO 20161014.4560870\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (03): (BK,HK) fatal 20160929.4525807\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (02): (BK) fatal 20160924.4512897\nMonkeypox, chimpanzee - Cameroon: (CE) emerging, OIE 20160827.4445967\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (02): (BU) 20160212.4014697\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic: (MB) RFI 20160119.3947756\n2015\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp. He presented to a local health facility on [Thu 16 Mar 2017] with fever, body pain, malaise, dysphagia, and enlarged cervical lymph nodes; and was presumptively treated for malaria and sore throat as an out-patient.\n\nOn [Fri 17 Mar 2017], he developed generalized vesicular skin eruptions in addition to the initial constitutional clinical features. The case-patient was admitted to Pujehun district hospital on [Sat 25 Mar 2017].\n\nLaboratory results from Institut National de Recherche Biomedicale (INRB) in Kinshasha, DRC last week [week of 16 April 2017] indicated that the vesicular swab tested positive for Orthopox virus by polymerase chain reaction (PCR) assay and monkeypox virus by GeneXpert technique. Based on these results, the Sierra Leone Ministry of Health notified WHO on [Mon 17 Apr 2017] of the outbreak of monkeypox.\n\nA total of 13 close contacts to the index case were listed and are being followed up. None of them have developed any febrile illness and/or skin lesions in the 1st 21 days since the last exposure. The contacts are still being monitored for additional 21 days (twice the maximum incubation period of monkeypox).\n\nThis is the 2nd sporadic occurrence of monkeypox in the country, with the 1st one reported in March 2014 in Bo district located in the southern region of the country.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Occurrence of sporadic cases is not surprising because monkeypox virus is endemic in West Africa, including Sierra Leone and human cases occur sporadically in the region.\n\nAs noted in previous ProMED-mail posts, the monkeypox virus clade in the Congo Basin causes more severe disease in humans, with a case fatality rate of 11-17 percent, than the clade in West Africa, which causes few fatalities. The virus is seldom transmitted directly from one person to another, but there is a documented instance of that in the Republic of the Congo. Monkeys are not the reservoirs of the virus, despite the name that the virus has received. Although not determined, the main reservoirs of monkeypox are suspected to be rodents, including rope squirrels (_Funisciurus_ sp; an arboreal rodent) and terrestrial rodents in the genera _Cricetomys_ and _Graphiurus_. - Mod.TY\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/46.]\nSee Also\nMonkeypox - Africa (02): Republic of the Congo 20170404.4947955\nMonkeypox - Africa: Republic of the Congo 20170315.4903754\n2016\n----\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (04): (HK) WHO 20161014.4560870\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (03): (BK,HK) fatal 20160929.4525807\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic (02): (BK) fatal 20160924.4512897\nMonkeypox, chimpanzee - Cameroon: (CE) emerging, OIE 20160827.4445967\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (02): (BU) 20160212.4014697\nMonkeypox - Central African Republic: (MB) RFI 20160119.3947756\n2015\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp.
