Africa (02): Republic of the Congo", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=4947955", "date": "2016-02-12 14:43:11", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX - CONGO DR (02): (BAS-UELE)\n*************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 10 Feb 2016\nSource: Outbreak News Today [edited]\nhttp://outbreaknewstoday.com/monkeypox-outbreak-dozens-of-cases-reported-in-bas-uele-district-drc-25667/\n\n\nHealth officials in Bas-Uele [province], [formerly a district of] Orientale province of northern Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC) are reporting a monkeypox outbreak, which has affected more than 50 and killed 2, according to a Radio Okapi report today [10 Feb 2016] ([in French], computer translated).\n\nDr Innocent Akonda said that 51 cases were reported during the 1st week of February [2016], with cases reported in the Bombongolo health area, Aboso, and other health areas. With the new cases, the health zone recorded 195 cases including 8 deaths, said Akonda.\n\nThe battle against the outbreak is especially problematic due to lack of qualified medical personnel, equipment, collection kits, and lack of sanitation. \"We only have one qualified nurse in Bombongolo. When he moves, it is the orderlies who care for the sick. Healers also monopolize the sick to offer them miracle healings, \" added Dr Innocent Akonda.\n\nMonkeypox is a relatively rare virus found primarily in central and western Africa. The disease is caused by monkeypox virus. It is closely related to the smallpox virus (variola), the virus used in the smallpox vaccine (vaccinia), and the cowpox virus. Infection with monkeypox is not as serious as its cousin, smallpox; however, human deaths have been attributed to monkeypox.\n\nAccording to the CDC, the symptoms of monkeypox are as follows:\n- About 12 days after people are infected with the virus, they will get a fever, headache, muscle aches, and backache; their lymph nodes will swell; and they will feel tired.\n- 1 to 3 days (or longer) after the fever starts, they will get a rash. This rash develops into raised bumps filled with fluid and often starts on the face and spreads, but it can start on other parts of the body too.\n- The bumps go through several stages before they get crusty, scab over, and fall off.\nThe illness usually lasts for 2 to 4 weeks.\n\nRodents, such as rope squirrels, door mice, and pouched rats, are the suspected reservoir hosts, with monkeys and humans as secondary, spill-over hosts. The monkeypox virus in the Congo has a higher case fatality rate than the virus in West Africa, where fatal cases are rare or absent, according to a ProMED moderator [Monkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp. 20120917.1297716].\n\nPeople at risk for monkeypox are those who get bitten by an infected animal or if you have contact with the animal's rash, blood, or body fluids. It can also be transmitted person to person through respiratory or direct contact and contact with contaminated bedding or clothing [this is extremely rare. - Mod.TY]. There is no specific treatment for monkeypox.\n\nA 2010 study published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [http://www.pnas.org/content/107/37/16262.full], says that since the last doses of smallpox vaccine were given in 1980 and smallpox was officially eradicated, cases of monkeypox have increased 20-fold according to the study.\n\nMuch of this is likely due to the immunity that smallpox vaccine granted to related viruses like monkeypox. Most of the patients infected with monkeypox were born after the discontinuation of smallpox vaccination.\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This report does not indicate if these 51 cases were laboratory confirmed. This area in the DRC is endemic for monkeypox virus, and sporadic human cases occur in this region.\n\nThe monkeypox virus clade in the Congo Basin causes more severe disease in humans, with a case fatality rate of 11-17 percent, than the clade in Ghana, which causes few fatalities. It was this latter clade that was introduced into the USA with the importation of African rodents in the pet trade, with resulting human cases in several states and no fatalities.\n\nMaps of the Democratic Republic of Congo can be seen at https://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/monkey-cage/files/2015/06/Decentralized-provinces-of-the-DRC-CDRC_DG002_122.jpg and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/33637. Bas-Uele, in northern DRC had a population of approximately 900 000 in 2007 (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bas-Uele_District). - Mod.TY]\nSee Also\n2015\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp. Infection with monkeypox is not as serious as its cousin, smallpox; however, human deaths have been attributed to monkeypox.\n\nAccording to the CDC, the symptoms of monkeypox are as follows:\n- About 12 days after people are infected with the virus, they will get a fever, headache, muscle aches, and backache; their lymph nodes will swell; and they will feel tired.\n- 1 to 3 days (or longer) after the fever starts, they will get a rash. This rash develops into raised bumps filled with fluid and often starts on the face and spreads, but it can start on other parts of the body too.\n- The bumps go through several stages before they get crusty, scab over, and fall off.\nThe illness usually lasts for 2 to 4 weeks.\n\nRodents, such as rope squirrels, door mice, and pouched rats, are the suspected reservoir hosts, with monkeys and humans as secondary, spill-over hosts. The monkeypox virus in the Congo has a higher case fatality rate than the virus in West Africa, where fatal cases are rare or absent, according to a ProMED moderator [Monkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp. 20120917.1297716].\n\nPeople at risk for monkeypox are those who get bitten by an infected animal or if you have contact with the animal's rash, blood, or body fluids. It can also be transmitted person to person through respiratory or direct contact and contact with contaminated bedding or clothing [this is extremely rare. - Mod.TY]. There is no specific treatment for monkeypox.\n\nA 2010 study published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [http://www.pnas.org/content/107/37/16262.full], says that since the last doses of smallpox vaccine were given in 1980 and smallpox was officially eradicated, cases of monkeypox have increased 20-fold according to the study.\n\nMuch of this is likely due to the immunity that smallpox vaccine granted to related viruses like monkeypox. Most of the patients infected with monkeypox were born after the discontinuation of smallpox vaccination.\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This report does not indicate if these 51 cases were laboratory confirmed. This area in the DRC is endemic for monkeypox virus, and sporadic human cases occur in this region.\n\nThe monkeypox virus clade in the Congo Basin causes more severe disease in humans, with a case fatality rate of 11-17 percent, than the clade in Ghana, which causes few fatalities. It was this latter clade that was introduced into the USA with the importation of African rodents in the pet trade, with resulting human cases in several states and no fatalities.\n\nMaps of the Democratic Republic of Congo can be seen at https://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/monkey-cage/files/2015/06/Decentralized-provinces-of-the-DRC-CDRC_DG002_122.jpg and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/33637. Bas-Uele, in northern DRC had a population of approximately 900 000 in 2007 (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bas-Uele_District). - Mod.TY]\nSee Also\n2015\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR: susp. RFI", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=3643887", "date": "2010-09-01 17:00:03", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX, INCREASED INCIDENCE - DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO\n*************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Tue 31 Aug 2010\nSource: Reuters Health [edited]\n<http://uk.reuters.com/article/idUKTRE67U4RH20100831>\n\n\nMonkeypox rising in wake of smallpox eradication\n------------------------------------------------\nSome 30 years after authorities doled out the\nlast dose of smallpox vaccine, the world faces\nanother multiplying menace: monkeypox. A new\nstudy suggests that the monkeypox virus, which\nthe smallpox vaccine also grants immunity\nagainst, is now at least 20 times as common as it\nwas shortly after victory over smallpox had been declared.\n\n\"The eradication of smallpox was one of the\ngreatest achievements known to man,\" lead\nresearcher Anne Rimoin of the University of\nCalifornia, Los Angeles School of Public Health\ntold Reuters Health. \"But a consequence of\nceasing smallpox vaccinations is that now the\nworld's population is vulnerable to other (related viruses) such as monkeypox.\"\n\nWhile the infection is somewhat less serious than\nsmallpox, it can still scar and even kill its\nvictims. And in contrast to its cousin, monkeypox\nis not only able to jump between humans, but can\ninfect through contact with small animals that\nharbor the virus. As a result, its control could\nbe all the more challenging, warned Rimoin.\n\nConverging political, social, economic and\nenvironmental factors make African nations -- in\nparticular, the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n-- especially vulnerable to the infection, she\nexplained. The virus's favorite animal hosts such\nas squirrels and monkeys are endemic [abundant]\nthere, and civil war has forced many people to\nrely heavily on hunting wildlife for sustenance.\nSome have even migrated deep into the animals'\nforest habitats to seek refuge from the violence.\n\n\"The virus has probably been on the rise for\nyears, but the country lacked surveillance,\"\nRimoin noted. \"To find disease, you have to look\nfor it.\" So she and her colleagues, who included\nmany local Congolese, did just that. Using\nChinese bicycles like pack mules to transport\nsupplies, and with funding from the U.S. National\nInstitutes of Health, they surveyed 9 local\nhealth zones for signs of monkeypox between\nNovember 2005 and November 2007. They identified\n760 cases of laboratory-confirmed monkeypox.\n\nCompared to similar surveillance conducted in the\n1980s, Rimoin's team found a 20-fold increase in\nmonkeypox cases -- far more than they ever\nexpected to find. In a single health zone, the\naverage number of yearly cases rose from less\nthan 1 to roughly 14 per 10 000 people.\n\nMost of the victims were born after smallpox\nvaccination was officially discontinued in 1980.\nVaccinated individuals were more than 5 times\nless likely to become infected with monkeypox\ncompared to those without the vaccine's\nprotection, the researchers report in the\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [Abstract reproduced below].\n\n\"What we're seeing is a harbinger of things to\ncome,\" said Rimoin. She warned that the virus\ncould grow more widespread with further\ndeforestation, continued movement of people from\nrural to urban areas, bushmeat trafficking and\nimportation of exotic pets. \"And every new\ninfection provides the virus with the opportunity\nto evolve into a more serious or transmissible virus,\" she added.\n\nIt's already clear that the Democratic Republic\nof the Congo isn't the only home for the virus.\nThe Republic of the Congo and Sudan also reported\ncases in recent years. And in 2003, monkeypox\narrived in the U.S. Midwest with imported African\nrodents, before spreading among prairie dogs and\nsickening 90 people. Experts fear an even more\nvirulent and efficient virus could return to the western world.\n\n\"The higher the rate of new infections, the\ngreater the chance that travelers from the U.S.\nwill be exposed, and that the disease will be\nimported into the U.S. -- possibly establishing\nitself in U.S. rodent populations,\" Dr. Dan\nDiGiulio of Stanford University School of\nMedicine in California, who was not involved in\nthe study, noted in an e-mail message to Reuters Health.\n\nSo what can be done to keep the virus at bay?\nRimoin suggested that behavioral interventions\nmay be the most effective strategy at this point,\nincluding teaching people at risk of infection\nwhat animals may be most likely to carry\nmonkeypox and how to handle them to avoid\ninfection, as well as isolating infected\nindividuals. Continued active surveillance is\nalso important to better identify the animal\nreservoirs and rates of animal-to-human versus\nhuman-to-human transmission. \"Once we understand\nmore about this virus and what it may mean for\nus,\" she said, \"we may be able to consider\nspecific interventions, perhaps vaccinating\ngroups that are at significant risk of\ninfection.\" DiGiulio added the need for animal\nimportation policies, and research into effective\nantiviral treatments and vaccine development.\n\n\"Three decades after the eradication of smallpox,\npox viruses still deserve our close attention,\"\nsaid Rimoin. \"And we shouldn't only worry about\nits accidental introduction but also as a deliberate terrorist release.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The following is the reference and abstract for\nthe publication discussed in the above press report.\n\nTitle: Major increase in human monkeypox\nincidence 30 years after smallpox vaccination\ncampaigns cease in the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nAuthors: Anne W. Rimoina and 18 others.\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences,\n2010; DOI:10.1073/pnas.1005769107, online edition.\n\nAbstract: \"Studies on the burden of human\nmonkeypox in the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n(DRC) were last conducted from 1981 to 1986.\nSince then, the population that is\nimmunologically naive to orthopoxviruses has\nincreased significantly due to cessation of mass\nsmallpox vaccination campaigns.\n\nTo assess the current risk of infection, we\nanalyzed human monkeypox incidence trends in a\nmonkeypox-enzootic region. Active,\npopulation-based surveillance was conducted in 9\nhealth zones in central DRC. Epidemiologic data\nand biological samples were obtained from\nsuspected cases. Cumulative incidence (per 10 000\npopulation) and major determinants of infection\nwere compared with data from active surveillance\nin similar regions from 1981 to 1986.\n\nBetween November 2005 and November 2007, 760\nlaboratory-confirmed human monkeypox cases were\nidentified in participating health zones. The\naverage annual cumulative incidence across zones\nwas 5.53 per 10 000 (2.18\u00ad14.42). Factors\nassociated with increased risk of infection\nincluded: living in forested areas, male gender,\nage <15, and no prior smallpox vaccination.\nVaccinated persons had a 5.2-fold lower risk of\nmonkeypox than unvaccinated persons (0.78 vs. 4.05 per 10,000).\n\nComparison of active surveillance data in the\nsame health zone from the 1980s (0.72 per 10 000)\nand 2006\u00ad07 (14.42 per 10 000) suggests a 20-fold\nincrease in human monkeypox incidence. 30 years\nafter mass smallpox vaccination campaigns ceased,\nhuman monkeypox incidence has dramatically\nincreased in rural DRC. Improved surveillance and\nepidemiological analysis is needed to better\nassess the public health burden and develop\nstrategies for reducing the risk of wider spread of infection.\"\n\nThe virus species _Monkeypox virus_ is a member\nof the genus _Orthopoxvirus_. Monkeypox virus can\ncauses a disease in humans that is clinically\nsimilar to smallpox and outbreaks in central\nAfrica have been associated with significant\nmortality, although none occurred in the outbreak\nin the USA in 2003 associated with introduced African rodents.\n\nAn important difference between monkeypox virus\nand smallpox virus (variola virus) is that\nmonkeypox virus has not transmitted from\nperson-to-person as efficiently as variola virus.\nIndeed monkeypox virus is normally resident in\nrodents and squirrels but can be transmitted to\nother species as zoonoses. Phylogenetically the\nspecies _Monkeypox virus_ is among the most\ndivergent viral species from the species _\nVariola virus_. Indeed variola virus is more\nclosely related to camelpox virus than to monkeypox virus.\n\nConsequently evolution of monkeypox virus into a\nvariola-like virus by mutation and selection is\nimprobable. Nonetheless it has potential to cause\nsignificant disease in the human population and\nmay require in limited situations reuse of vaccinia virus vaccine.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo can be accessed at:\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01Cl>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2008\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Bokungu) 20080714.2141\n2006\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR (Kasai Occidental): RFI 20061023.3040\n2005\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Mbuji-Mayi) 20050509.1276\n2002\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (05) 20020409.3918\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (04) 20020322.3797\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (03) 20020315.3749\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20020314.3744\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n----\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n...................cp/ejp/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Using\nChinese bicycles like pack mules to transport\nsupplies, and with funding from the U.S. National\nInstitutes of Health, they surveyed 9 local\nhealth zones for signs of monkeypox between\nNovember 2005 and November 2007. They identified\n760 cases of laboratory-confirmed monkeypox.\n\nCompared to similar surveillance conducted in the\n1980s, Rimoin's team found a 20-fold increase in\nmonkeypox cases -- far more than they ever\nexpected to find. In a single health zone, the\naverage number of yearly cases rose from less\nthan 1 to roughly 14 per 10 000 people.\n\nMost of the victims were born after smallpox\nvaccination was officially discontinued in 1980.\nVaccinated individuals were more than 5 times\nless likely to become infected with monkeypox\ncompared to those without the vaccine's\nprotection, the researchers report in the\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [Abstract reproduced below].\n\n\"What we're seeing is a harbinger of things to\ncome,\" said Rimoin. She warned that the virus\ncould grow more widespread with further\ndeforestation, continued movement of people from\nrural to urban areas, bushmeat trafficking and\nimportation of exotic pets. \"And every new\ninfection provides the virus with the opportunity\nto evolve into a more serious or transmissible virus,\" she added.\n\nIt's already clear that the Democratic Republic\nof the Congo isn't the only home for the virus.\nThe Republic of the Congo and Sudan also reported\ncases in recent years. And in 2003, monkeypox\narrived in the U.S. Midwest with imported African\nrodents, before spreading among prairie dogs and\nsickening 90 people. Experts fear an even more\nvirulent and efficient virus could return to the western world.\n\n\"The higher the rate of new infections, the\ngreater the chance that travelers from the U.S.\nwill be exposed, and that the disease will be\nimported into the U.S. -- possibly establishing\nitself in U.S. rodent populations,\" Dr. Dan\nDiGiulio of Stanford University School of\nMedicine in California, who was not involved in\nthe study, noted in an e-mail message to Reuters Health.\n\nSo what can be done to keep the virus at bay?\nRimoin suggested that behavioral interventions\nmay be the most effective strategy at this point,\nincluding teaching people at risk of infection\nwhat animals may be most likely to carry\nmonkeypox and how to handle them to avoid\ninfection, as well as isolating infected\nindividuals. Continued active surveillance is\nalso important to better identify the animal\nreservoirs and rates of animal-to-human versus\nhuman-to-human transmission. \"Once we understand\nmore about this virus and what it may mean for\nus,\" she said, \"we may be able to consider\nspecific interventions, perhaps vaccinating\ngroups that are at significant risk of\ninfection.\" DiGiulio added the need for animal\nimportation policies, and research into effective\nantiviral treatments and vaccine development.\n\n\"Three decades after the eradication of smallpox,\npox viruses still deserve our close attention,\"\nsaid Rimoin. \"And we shouldn't only worry about\nits accidental introduction but also as a deliberate terrorist release.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The following is the reference and abstract for\nthe publication discussed in the above press report.\n\nTitle: Major increase in human monkeypox\nincidence 30 years after smallpox vaccination\ncampaigns cease in the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nAuthors: Anne W. Rimoina and 18 others.\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences,\n2010; DOI:10.1073/pnas.1005769107, online edition.\n\nAbstract: \"Studies on the burden of human\nmonkeypox in the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n(DRC) were last conducted from 1981 to 1986.\nSince then, the population that is\nimmunologically naive to orthopoxviruses has\nincreased significantly due to cessation of mass\nsmallpox vaccination campaigns.\n\nTo assess the current risk of infection, we\nanalyzed human monkeypox incidence trends in a\nmonkeypox-enzootic region. Active,\npopulation-based surveillance was conducted in 9\nhealth zones in central DRC. Epidemiologic data\nand biological samples were obtained from\nsuspected cases. Cumulative incidence (per 10 000\npopulation) and major determinants of infection\nwere compared with data from active surveillance\nin similar regions from 1981 to 1986.\n\nBetween November 2005 and November 2007, 760\nlaboratory-confirmed human monkeypox cases were\nidentified in participating health zones. The\naverage annual cumulative incidence across zones\nwas 5.53 per 10 000 (2.18\u00ad14.42). Factors\nassociated with increased risk of infection\nincluded: living in forested areas, male gender,\nage <15, and no prior smallpox vaccination.\nVaccinated persons had a 5.2-fold lower risk of\nmonkeypox than unvaccinated persons (0.78 vs. 4.05 per 10,000).\n\nComparison of active surveillance data in the\nsame health zone from the 1980s (0.72 per 10 000)\nand 2006\u00ad07 (14.42 per 10 000) suggests a 20-fold\nincrease in human monkeypox incidence. 30 years\nafter mass smallpox vaccination campaigns ceased,\nhuman monkeypox incidence has dramatically\nincreased in rural DRC. Improved surveillance and\nepidemiological analysis is needed to better\nassess the public health burden and develop\nstrategies for reducing the risk of wider spread of infection.\"\n\nThe virus species _Monkeypox virus_ is a member\nof the genus _Orthopoxvirus_. Monkeypox virus can\ncauses a disease in humans that is clinically\nsimilar to smallpox and outbreaks in central\nAfrica have been associated with significant\nmortality, although none occurred in the outbreak\nin the USA in 2003 associated with introduced African rodents.\n\nAn important difference between monkeypox virus\nand smallpox virus (variola virus) is that\nmonkeypox virus has not transmitted from\nperson-to-person as efficiently as variola virus.\nIndeed monkeypox virus is normally resident in\nrodents and squirrels but can be transmitted to\nother species as zoonoses. Phylogenetically the\nspecies _Monkeypox virus_ is among the most\ndivergent viral species from the species _\nVariola virus_. Indeed variola virus is more\nclosely related to camelpox virus than to monkeypox virus.\n\nConsequently evolution of monkeypox virus into a\nvariola-like virus by mutation and selection is\nimprobable. Nonetheless it has potential to cause\nsignificant disease in the human population and\nmay require in limited situations reuse of vaccinia virus vaccine.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo can be accessed at:\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01Cl>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2008\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Bokungu) 20080714.2141\n2006\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR (Kasai Occidental): RFI 20061023.3040\n2005\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Mbuji-Mayi) 20050509.1276\n2002\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (05) 20020409.3918\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (04) 20020322.3797\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (03) 20020315.3749\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20020314.3744\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n----\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n...................cp/ejp/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Congo DR: susp.", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1297716", "date": "2010-09-01 17:00:03", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX, INCREASED INCIDENCE - DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO\n*************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Tue 31 Aug 2010\nSource: Reuters Health [edited]\n<http://uk.reuters.com/article/idUKTRE67U4RH20100831>\n\n\nMonkeypox rising in wake of smallpox eradication\n------------------------------------------------\nSome 30 years after authorities doled out the\nlast dose of smallpox vaccine, the world faces\nanother multiplying menace: monkeypox. A new\nstudy suggests that the monkeypox virus, which\nthe smallpox vaccine also grants immunity\nagainst, is now at least 20 times as common as it\nwas shortly after victory over smallpox had been declared.\n\n\"The eradication of smallpox was one of the\ngreatest achievements known to man,\" lead\nresearcher Anne Rimoin of the University of\nCalifornia, Los Angeles School of Public Health\ntold Reuters Health. \"But a consequence of\nceasing smallpox vaccinations is that now the\nworld's population is vulnerable to other (related viruses) such as monkeypox.\"\n\nWhile the infection is somewhat less serious than\nsmallpox, it can still scar and even kill its\nvictims. And in contrast to its cousin, monkeypox\nis not only able to jump between humans, but can\ninfect through contact with small animals that\nharbor the virus. As a result, its control could\nbe all the more challenging, warned Rimoin.\n\nConverging political, social, economic and\nenvironmental factors make African nations -- in\nparticular, the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n-- especially vulnerable to the infection, she\nexplained. The virus's favorite animal hosts such\nas squirrels and monkeys are endemic [abundant]\nthere, and civil war has forced many people to\nrely heavily on hunting wildlife for sustenance.\nSome have even migrated deep into the animals'\nforest habitats to seek refuge from the violence.\n\n\"The virus has probably been on the rise for\nyears, but the country lacked surveillance,\"\nRimoin noted. \"To find disease, you have to look\nfor it.\" So she and her colleagues, who included\nmany local Congolese, did just that. Using\nChinese bicycles like pack mules to transport\nsupplies, and with funding from the U.S. National\nInstitutes of Health, they surveyed 9 local\nhealth zones for signs of monkeypox between\nNovember 2005 and November 2007. They identified\n760 cases of laboratory-confirmed monkeypox.\n\nCompared to similar surveillance conducted in the\n1980s, Rimoin's team found a 20-fold increase in\nmonkeypox cases -- far more than they ever\nexpected to find. In a single health zone, the\naverage number of yearly cases rose from less\nthan 1 to roughly 14 per 10 000 people.\n\nMost of the victims were born after smallpox\nvaccination was officially discontinued in 1980.\nVaccinated individuals were more than 5 times\nless likely to become infected with monkeypox\ncompared to those without the vaccine's\nprotection, the researchers report in the\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [Abstract reproduced below].\n\n\"What we're seeing is a harbinger of things to\ncome,\" said Rimoin. She warned that the virus\ncould grow more widespread with further\ndeforestation, continued movement of people from\nrural to urban areas, bushmeat trafficking and\nimportation of exotic pets. \"And every new\ninfection provides the virus with the opportunity\nto evolve into a more serious or transmissible virus,\" she added.\n\nIt's already clear that the Democratic Republic\nof the Congo isn't the only home for the virus.\nThe Republic of the Congo and Sudan also reported\ncases in recent years. And in 2003, monkeypox\narrived in the U.S. Midwest with imported African\nrodents, before spreading among prairie dogs and\nsickening 90 people. Experts fear an even more\nvirulent and efficient virus could return to the western world.\n\n\"The higher the rate of new infections, the\ngreater the chance that travelers from the U.S.\nwill be exposed, and that the disease will be\nimported into the U.S. -- possibly establishing\nitself in U.S. rodent populations,\" Dr. Dan\nDiGiulio of Stanford University School of\nMedicine in California, who was not involved in\nthe study, noted in an e-mail message to Reuters Health.\n\nSo what can be done to keep the virus at bay?\nRimoin suggested that behavioral interventions\nmay be the most effective strategy at this point,\nincluding teaching people at risk of infection\nwhat animals may be most likely to carry\nmonkeypox and how to handle them to avoid\ninfection, as well as isolating infected\nindividuals. Continued active surveillance is\nalso important to better identify the animal\nreservoirs and rates of animal-to-human versus\nhuman-to-human transmission. \"Once we understand\nmore about this virus and what it may mean for\nus,\" she said, \"we may be able to consider\nspecific interventions, perhaps vaccinating\ngroups that are at significant risk of\ninfection.\" DiGiulio added the need for animal\nimportation policies, and research into effective\nantiviral treatments and vaccine development.\n\n\"Three decades after the eradication of smallpox,\npox viruses still deserve our close attention,\"\nsaid Rimoin. \"And we shouldn't only worry about\nits accidental introduction but also as a deliberate terrorist release.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The following is the reference and abstract for\nthe publication discussed in the above press report.\n\nTitle: Major increase in human monkeypox\nincidence 30 years after smallpox vaccination\ncampaigns cease in the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nAuthors: Anne W. Rimoina and 18 others.\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences,\n2010; DOI:10.1073/pnas.1005769107, online edition.\n\nAbstract: \"Studies on the burden of human\nmonkeypox in the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n(DRC) were last conducted from 1981 to 1986.\nSince then, the population that is\nimmunologically naive to orthopoxviruses has\nincreased significantly due to cessation of mass\nsmallpox vaccination campaigns.\n\nTo assess the current risk of infection, we\nanalyzed human monkeypox incidence trends in a\nmonkeypox-enzootic region. Cumulative incidence (per 10 000\npopulation) and major determinants of infection\nwere compared with data from active surveillance\nin similar regions from 1981 to 1986.\n\nBetween November 2005 and November 2007, 760\nlaboratory-confirmed human monkeypox cases were\nidentified in participating health zones. The\naverage annual cumulative incidence across zones\nwas 5.53 per 10 000 (2.18\u00ad14.42). Indeed variola virus is more\nclosely related to camelpox virus than to monkeypox virus.\n\nConsequently evolution of monkeypox virus into a\nvariola-like virus by mutation and selection is\nimprobable. Improved surveillance and\nepidemiological analysis is needed to better\nassess the public health burden and develop\nstrategies for reducing the risk of wider spread of infection.\"\n\nThe virus species _Monkeypox virus_ is a member\nof the genus _Orthopoxvirus_. Monkeypox virus can\ncauses a disease in humans that is clinically\nsimilar to smallpox and outbreaks in central\nAfrica have been associated with significant\nmortality, although none occurred in the outbreak\nin the USA in 2003 associated with introduced African rodents.\n\nAn important difference between monkeypox virus\nand smallpox virus (variola virus) is that\nmonkeypox virus has not transmitted from\nperson-to-person as efficiently as variola virus.\nIndeed monkeypox virus is normally resident in\nrodents and squirrels but can be transmitted to\nother species as zoonoses. Phylogenetically the\nspecies _Monkeypox virus_ is among the most\ndivergent viral species from the species _\nVariola virus_. Indeed variola virus is more\nclosely related to camelpox virus than to monkeypox virus.\n\nConsequently evolution of monkeypox virus into a\nvariola-like virus by mutation and selection is\nimprobable. Nonetheless it has potential to cause\nsignificant disease in the human population and\nmay require in limited situations reuse of vaccinia virus vaccine.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo can be accessed at:\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01Cl>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2008\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Bokungu) 20080714.2141\n2006\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR (Kasai Occidental): RFI 20061023.3040\n2005\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Mbuji-Mayi) 20050509.1276\n2002\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (05) 20020409.3918\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (04) 20020322.3797\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (03) 20020315.3749\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20020314.3744\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n----\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n...................cp/ejp/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Congo DR (02): (ET)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=658819", "date": "2010-09-01 17:00:03", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX, INCREASED INCIDENCE - DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO\n*************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Tue 31 Aug 2010\nSource: Reuters Health [edited]\n<http://uk.reuters.com/article/idUKTRE67U4RH20100831>\n\n\nMonkeypox rising in wake of smallpox eradication\n------------------------------------------------\nSome 30 years after authorities doled out the\nlast dose of smallpox vaccine, the world faces\nanother multiplying menace: monkeypox. A new\nstudy suggests that the monkeypox virus, which\nthe smallpox vaccine also grants immunity\nagainst, is now at least 20 times as common as it\nwas shortly after victory over smallpox had been declared.\n\n\"The eradication of smallpox was one of the\ngreatest achievements known to man,\" lead\nresearcher Anne Rimoin of the University of\nCalifornia, Los Angeles School of Public Health\ntold Reuters Health. \"But a consequence of\nceasing smallpox vaccinations is that now the\nworld's population is vulnerable to other (related viruses) such as monkeypox.\"\n\nWhile the infection is somewhat less serious than\nsmallpox, it can still scar and even kill its\nvictims. And in contrast to its cousin, monkeypox\nis not only able to jump between humans, but can\ninfect through contact with small animals that\nharbor the virus. As a result, its control could\nbe all the more challenging, warned Rimoin.\n\nConverging political, social, economic and\nenvironmental factors make African nations -- in\nparticular, the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n-- especially vulnerable to the infection, she\nexplained. The virus's favorite animal hosts such\nas squirrels and monkeys are endemic [abundant]\nthere, and civil war has forced many people to\nrely heavily on hunting wildlife for sustenance.\nSome have even migrated deep into the animals'\nforest habitats to seek refuge from the violence.\n\n\"The virus has probably been on the rise for\nyears, but the country lacked surveillance,\"\nRimoin noted. \"To find disease, you have to look\nfor it.\" So she and her colleagues, who included\nmany local Congolese, did just that. Using\nChinese bicycles like pack mules to transport\nsupplies, and with funding from the U.S. National\nInstitutes of Health, they surveyed 9 local\nhealth zones for signs of monkeypox between\nNovember 2005 and November 2007. They identified\n760 cases of laboratory-confirmed monkeypox.\n\nCompared to similar surveillance conducted in the\n1980s, Rimoin's team found a 20-fold increase in\nmonkeypox cases -- far more than they ever\nexpected to find. In a single health zone, the\naverage number of yearly cases rose from less\nthan 1 to roughly 14 per 10 000 people.\n\nMost of the victims were born after smallpox\nvaccination was officially discontinued in 1980.\nVaccinated individuals were more than 5 times\nless likely to become infected with monkeypox\ncompared to those without the vaccine's\nprotection, the researchers report in the\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [Abstract reproduced below].\n\n\"What we're seeing is a harbinger of things to\ncome,\" said Rimoin. She warned that the virus\ncould grow more widespread with further\ndeforestation, continued movement of people from\nrural to urban areas, bushmeat trafficking and\nimportation of exotic pets. \"And every new\ninfection provides the virus with the opportunity\nto evolve into a more serious or transmissible virus,\" she added.\n\nIt's already clear that the Democratic Republic\nof the Congo isn't the only home for the virus.\nThe Republic of the Congo and Sudan also reported\ncases in recent years. And in 2003, monkeypox\narrived in the U.S. Midwest with imported African\nrodents, before spreading among prairie dogs and\nsickening 90 people. Experts fear an even more\nvirulent and efficient virus could return to the western world.\n\n\"The higher the rate of new infections, the\ngreater the chance that travelers from the U.S.\nwill be exposed, and that the disease will be\nimported into the U.S. -- possibly establishing\nitself in U.S. rodent populations,\" Dr. Dan\nDiGiulio of Stanford University School of\nMedicine in California, who was not involved in\nthe study, noted in an e-mail message to Reuters Health.\n\nSo what can be done to keep the virus at bay?\nRimoin suggested that behavioral interventions\nmay be the most effective strategy at this point,\nincluding teaching people at risk of infection\nwhat animals may be most likely to carry\nmonkeypox and how to handle them to avoid\ninfection, as well as isolating infected\nindividuals. Continued active surveillance is\nalso important to better identify the animal\nreservoirs and rates of animal-to-human versus\nhuman-to-human transmission. \"Once we understand\nmore about this virus and what it may mean for\nus,\" she said, \"we may be able to consider\nspecific interventions, perhaps vaccinating\ngroups that are at significant risk of\ninfection.\" DiGiulio added the need for animal\nimportation policies, and research into effective\nantiviral treatments and vaccine development.\n\n\"Three decades after the eradication of smallpox,\npox viruses still deserve our close attention,\"\nsaid Rimoin. \"And we shouldn't only worry about\nits accidental introduction but also as a deliberate terrorist release.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The following is the reference and abstract for\nthe publication discussed in the above press report.\n\nTitle: Major increase in human monkeypox\nincidence 30 years after smallpox vaccination\ncampaigns cease in the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nAuthors: Anne W. Rimoina and 18 others.\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences,\n2010; DOI:10.1073/pnas.1005769107, online edition.\n\nAbstract: \"Studies on the burden of human\nmonkeypox in the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n(DRC) were last conducted from 1981 to 1986.\nSince then, the population that is\nimmunologically naive to orthopoxviruses has\nincreased significantly due to cessation of mass\nsmallpox vaccination campaigns.\n\nTo assess the current risk of infection, we\nanalyzed human monkeypox incidence trends in a\nmonkeypox-enzootic region. Active,\npopulation-based surveillance was conducted in 9\nhealth zones in central DRC. Epidemiologic data\nand biological samples were obtained from\nsuspected cases. Cumulative incidence (per 10 000\npopulation) and major determinants of infection\nwere compared with data from active surveillance\nin similar regions from 1981 to 1986.\n\nBetween November 2005 and November 2007, 760\nlaboratory-confirmed human monkeypox cases were\nidentified in participating health zones. The\naverage annual cumulative incidence across zones\nwas 5.53 per 10 000 (2.18\u00ad14.42). Factors\nassociated with increased risk of infection\nincluded: living in forested areas, male gender,\nage <15, and no prior smallpox vaccination.\nVaccinated persons had a 5.2-fold lower risk of\nmonkeypox than unvaccinated persons (0.78 vs. 4.05 per 10,000).\n\nComparison of active surveillance data in the\nsame health zone from the 1980s (0.72 per 10 000)\nand 2006\u00ad07 (14.42 per 10 000) suggests a 20-fold\nincrease in human monkeypox incidence. 30 years\nafter mass smallpox vaccination campaigns ceased,\nhuman monkeypox incidence has dramatically\nincreased in rural DRC. Improved surveillance and\nepidemiological analysis is needed to better\nassess the public health burden and develop\nstrategies for reducing the risk of wider spread of infection.\"\n\nThe virus species _Monkeypox virus_ is a member\nof the genus _Orthopoxvirus_. Monkeypox virus can\ncauses a disease in humans that is clinically\nsimilar to smallpox and outbreaks in central\nAfrica have been associated with significant\nmortality, although none occurred in the outbreak\nin the USA in 2003 associated with introduced African rodents.\n\nAn important difference between monkeypox virus\nand smallpox virus (variola virus) is that\nmonkeypox virus has not transmitted from\nperson-to-person as efficiently as variola virus.\nIndeed monkeypox virus is normally resident in\nrodents and squirrels but can be transmitted to\nother species as zoonoses. Phylogenetically the\nspecies _Monkeypox virus_ is among the most\ndivergent viral species from the species _\nVariola virus_. Indeed variola virus is more\nclosely related to camelpox virus than to monkeypox virus.\n\nConsequently evolution of monkeypox virus into a\nvariola-like virus by mutation and selection is\nimprobable. Nonetheless it has potential to cause\nsignificant disease in the human population and\nmay require in limited situations reuse of vaccinia virus vaccine.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo can be accessed at:\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01Cl>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2008\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Bokungu) 20080714.2141\n2006\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR (Kasai Occidental): RFI 20061023.3040\n2005\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Mbuji-Mayi) 20050509.1276\n2002\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (05) 20020409.3918\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (04) 20020322.3797\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (03) 20020315.3749\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20020314.3744\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n----\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n...................cp/ejp/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. You may unsub-\nscribe at <http://www.isid.org/promedmail/subscribe.lasso>.\nFor assistance from a human being, send mail to:\n<postmaster@promedmail.org>.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "smallpox", "monkeypox"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2010-03-16 00:00:00", "2005-11-16 00:00:00", "2007-11-16 00:00:00", "1980-03-16 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-03-16 00:00:00", "1981-03-16 00:00:00", "1986-03-16 00:00:00", "1981-03-16 00:00:00", "1986-03-16 00:00:00", "2007-11-16 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2006-03-16 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "the Democratic Republic of the Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the Democratic Republic", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "U.S.", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "California", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the \n Democratic Republic of Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Sudan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the Democratic Republic of Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "the Republic of the Congo", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Rimoin", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "513411", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox, increased incidence - Congo DR", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=513411", "date": "2010-09-01 17:00:03", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX, INCREASED INCIDENCE - DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC OF CONGO\n*************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Tue 31 Aug 2010\nSource: Reuters Health [edited]\n<http://uk.reuters.com/article/idUKTRE67U4RH20100831>\n\n\nMonkeypox rising in wake of smallpox eradication\n------------------------------------------------\nSome 30 years after authorities doled out the\nlast dose of smallpox vaccine, the world faces\nanother multiplying menace: monkeypox. A new\nstudy suggests that the monkeypox virus, which\nthe smallpox vaccine also grants immunity\nagainst, is now at least 20 times as common as it\nwas shortly after victory over smallpox had been declared.\n\n\"The eradication of smallpox was one of the\ngreatest achievements known to man,\" lead\nresearcher Anne Rimoin of the University of\nCalifornia, Los Angeles School of Public Health\ntold Reuters Health. \"But a consequence of\nceasing smallpox vaccinations is that now the\nworld's population is vulnerable to other (related viruses) such as monkeypox.\"\n\nWhile the infection is somewhat less serious than\nsmallpox, it can still scar and even kill its\nvictims. And in contrast to its cousin, monkeypox\nis not only able to jump between humans, but can\ninfect through contact with small animals that\nharbor the virus. As a result, its control could\nbe all the more challenging, warned Rimoin.\n\nConverging political, social, economic and\nenvironmental factors make African nations -- in\nparticular, the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n-- especially vulnerable to the infection, she\nexplained. The virus's favorite animal hosts such\nas squirrels and monkeys are endemic [abundant]\nthere, and civil war has forced many people to\nrely heavily on hunting wildlife for sustenance.\nSome have even migrated deep into the animals'\nforest habitats to seek refuge from the violence.\n\n\"The virus has probably been on the rise for\nyears, but the country lacked surveillance,\"\nRimoin noted. \"To find disease, you have to look\nfor it.\" So she and her colleagues, who included\nmany local Congolese, did just that. Using\nChinese bicycles like pack mules to transport\nsupplies, and with funding from the U.S. National\nInstitutes of Health, they surveyed 9 local\nhealth zones for signs of monkeypox between\nNovember 2005 and November 2007. They identified\n760 cases of laboratory-confirmed monkeypox.\n\nCompared to similar surveillance conducted in the\n1980s, Rimoin's team found a 20-fold increase in\nmonkeypox cases -- far more than they ever\nexpected to find. In a single health zone, the\naverage number of yearly cases rose from less\nthan 1 to roughly 14 per 10 000 people.\n\nMost of the victims were born after smallpox\nvaccination was officially discontinued in 1980.\nVaccinated individuals were more than 5 times\nless likely to become infected with monkeypox\ncompared to those without the vaccine's\nprotection, the researchers report in the\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences [Abstract reproduced below].\n\n\"What we're seeing is a harbinger of things to\ncome,\" said Rimoin. She warned that the virus\ncould grow more widespread with further\ndeforestation, continued movement of people from\nrural to urban areas, bushmeat trafficking and\nimportation of exotic pets. \"And every new\ninfection provides the virus with the opportunity\nto evolve into a more serious or transmissible virus,\" she added.\n\nIt's already clear that the Democratic Republic\nof the Congo isn't the only home for the virus.\nThe Republic of the Congo and Sudan also reported\ncases in recent years. And in 2003, monkeypox\narrived in the U.S. Midwest with imported African\nrodents, before spreading among prairie dogs and\nsickening 90 people. Experts fear an even more\nvirulent and efficient virus could return to the western world.\n\n\"The higher the rate of new infections, the\ngreater the chance that travelers from the U.S.\nwill be exposed, and that the disease will be\nimported into the U.S. -- possibly establishing\nitself in U.S. rodent populations,\" Dr. Dan\nDiGiulio of Stanford University School of\nMedicine in California, who was not involved in\nthe study, noted in an e-mail message to Reuters Health.\n\nSo what can be done to keep the virus at bay?\nRimoin suggested that behavioral interventions\nmay be the most effective strategy at this point,\nincluding teaching people at risk of infection\nwhat animals may be most likely to carry\nmonkeypox and how to handle them to avoid\ninfection, as well as isolating infected\nindividuals. Continued active surveillance is\nalso important to better identify the animal\nreservoirs and rates of animal-to-human versus\nhuman-to-human transmission. \"Once we understand\nmore about this virus and what it may mean for\nus,\" she said, \"we may be able to consider\nspecific interventions, perhaps vaccinating\ngroups that are at significant risk of\ninfection.\" DiGiulio added the need for animal\nimportation policies, and research into effective\nantiviral treatments and vaccine development.\n\n\"Three decades after the eradication of smallpox,\npox viruses still deserve our close attention,\"\nsaid Rimoin. \"And we shouldn't only worry about\nits accidental introduction but also as a deliberate terrorist release.\"\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The following is the reference and abstract for\nthe publication discussed in the above press report.\n\nTitle: Major increase in human monkeypox\nincidence 30 years after smallpox vaccination\ncampaigns cease in the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nAuthors: Anne W. Rimoina and 18 others.\nProceedings of the National Academy of Sciences,\n2010; DOI:10.1073/pnas.1005769107, online edition.\n\nAbstract: \"Studies on the burden of human\nmonkeypox in the Democratic Republic of the Congo\n(DRC) were last conducted from 1981 to 1986.\nSince then, the population that is\nimmunologically naive to orthopoxviruses has\nincreased significantly due to cessation of mass\nsmallpox vaccination campaigns.\n\nTo assess the current risk of infection, we\nanalyzed human monkeypox incidence trends in a\nmonkeypox-enzootic region. Active,\npopulation-based surveillance was conducted in 9\nhealth zones in central DRC. Epidemiologic data\nand biological samples were obtained from\nsuspected cases. Cumulative incidence (per 10 000\npopulation) and major determinants of infection\nwere compared with data from active surveillance\nin similar regions from 1981 to 1986.\n\nBetween November 2005 and November 2007, 760\nlaboratory-confirmed human monkeypox cases were\nidentified in participating health zones. The\naverage annual cumulative incidence across zones\nwas 5.53 per 10 000 (2.18\u00ad14.42). Factors\nassociated with increased risk of infection\nincluded: living in forested areas, male gender,\nage <15, and no prior smallpox vaccination.\nVaccinated persons had a 5.2-fold lower risk of\nmonkeypox than unvaccinated persons (0.78 vs. 4.05 per 10,000).\n\nComparison of active surveillance data in the\nsame health zone from the 1980s (0.72 per 10 000)\nand 2006\u00ad07 (14.42 per 10 000) suggests a 20-fold\nincrease in human monkeypox incidence. 30 years\nafter mass smallpox vaccination campaigns ceased,\nhuman monkeypox incidence has dramatically\nincreased in rural DRC. Improved surveillance and\nepidemiological analysis is needed to better\nassess the public health burden and develop\nstrategies for reducing the risk of wider spread of infection.\"\n\nThe virus species _Monkeypox virus_ is a member\nof the genus _Orthopoxvirus_. Monkeypox virus can\ncauses a disease in humans that is clinically\nsimilar to smallpox and outbreaks in central\nAfrica have been associated with significant\nmortality, although none occurred in the outbreak\nin the USA in 2003 associated with introduced African rodents.\n\nAn important difference between monkeypox virus\nand smallpox virus (variola virus) is that\nmonkeypox virus has not transmitted from\nperson-to-person as efficiently as variola virus.\nIndeed monkeypox virus is normally resident in\nrodents and squirrels but can be transmitted to\nother species as zoonoses. Phylogenetically the\nspecies _Monkeypox virus_ is among the most\ndivergent viral species from the species _\nVariola virus_. Indeed variola virus is more\nclosely related to camelpox virus than to monkeypox virus.\n\nConsequently evolution of monkeypox virus into a\nvariola-like virus by mutation and selection is\nimprobable. Nonetheless it has potential to cause\nsignificant disease in the human population and\nmay require in limited situations reuse of vaccinia virus vaccine.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo can be accessed at:\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01Cl>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2008\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Bokungu) 20080714.2141\n2006\n----\nMonkeypox, human - Congo DR (Kasai Occidental): RFI 20061023.3040\n2005\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Mbuji-Mayi) 20050509.1276\n2002\n----\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (05) 20020409.3918\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (04) 20020322.3797\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (03) 20020315.3749\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20020314.3744\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n----\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n...................cp/ejp/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. CONGO DR (MBUJI-MAYI)\n*********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Mon 9 May 2005\nFrom: Shirley Bannatyne <shirley.bannatyne@masta.org>\nSource: Agence France Presse News Agency, via MASTA [edited]\n\n\nDemocratic Republic of Congo: monkeypox outbreak in South-Central region\n------------------------------------------------\nA monkeypox virus epidemic has been reported near Mbuji Mayi [which is\nlocated in the South-Central region of the Democratic Republic of Congo].\nMore than 50 people have been [treated at the] Kanama health center, but no\ndeaths have been reported.\nThe provincial health inspection office has reported 56 cases, which\ninclude 14 children of less than 5 years and 2 of other age groups. All\nthese victims are from Kanama in the Kabinda District of the central region\nof the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n--\nShirley Bannatyne\nMASTA (Medical Advisory Service for Travellers Abroad)\n<shirley.bannatyne@masta.org>\n[Human infection with monkeypox virus has been reported in rural areas of\nthe tropical rain forests of west and central Africa, especially in the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo. The signs and symptoms are similar to those\nof smallpox, with generalized pustular rash, fever, and toxemia. The\ndisease, which is rare, became apparent only after the eradication of\nsmallpox. Monkeypox virus is a zoonotic agent and is acquired by humans\nthrough direct contact with the presumptive natural hosts: squirrels and\nmonkeys. Person-to-person transmission has been recorded, but the secondary\nattack rate is low. Nonetheless, the fear has been expressed that monkeypox\nmight evolve to become an endemic human infection. Determination of the\ncomplete sequence of the genome of monkeypox virus, however, has indicated\nthat monkeypox virus lacks some of the immunomodulatory genes possessed by\nsmallpox virus, which suggests that monkeypox virus is unlikely to become\nendemic in the human population.\nIn a comment relating to an outbreak in the same region of the Democratic\nRepublic of Congo in the year 2000 (see: Monkeypox - Congo, Dem. Rep.\n(Mbuji-Mayi): comment 20000506.069), Francois Merouze wrote that:\n\"Monkeypox is a zoonosis among African tree squirrels (_Heliosciurus_,\n_Funisciurus_). Human cases happen by direct contacts with sick animals.\nHuman to human subsequent cases are not very frequent. The usual mode for\nthis disease is anademic. There is no really prove[n] example of an\nimportant outbreak of monkeypox. The clinical diagnosis of monkeypox is not\nso easy, and chickenpox can be mistaken for monkeypox. Moreover, varicella\n(chickenpox), which is considered as an ordinary mild disease, can wear a\nvery different clinical aspect and even be a fatal disease for an\nimmunodepressed organism. In DRC, the prevalence rate of HIV is quite high.\"\nIt would be relevant to know whether chickenpox virus (a herpesvirus) has\nbeen excluded as the possible cause of this outbreak, since chickenpox is a\nvaccine-preventable disease. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (03) 20030710.1687\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (02): confirmed 20030626.1573\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep: suspected 20030623.1545\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (15) 20030710.1692\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem. Rep.\n(Mbuji-Mayi): comment 20000506.069), Francois Merouze wrote that:\n\"Monkeypox is a zoonosis among African tree squirrels (_Heliosciurus_,\n_Funisciurus_). There is no really prove[n] example of an\nimportant outbreak of monkeypox. The clinical diagnosis of monkeypox is not\nso easy, and chickenpox can be mistaken for monkeypox. Moreover, varicella\n(chickenpox), which is considered as an ordinary mild disease, can wear a\nvery different clinical aspect and even be a fatal disease for an\nimmunodepressed organism. In DRC, the prevalence rate of HIV is quite high.\"\nIt would be relevant to know whether chickenpox virus (a herpesvirus) has\nbeen excluded as the possible cause of this outbreak, since chickenpox is a\nvaccine-preventable disease. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (03) 20030710.1687\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (02): confirmed 20030626.1573\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep: suspected 20030623.1545\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (15) 20030710.1692\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem. All\nthese victims are from Kanama in the Kabinda District of the central region\nof the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n--\nShirley Bannatyne\nMASTA (Medical Advisory Service for Travellers Abroad)\n<shirley.bannatyne@masta.org>\n[Human infection with monkeypox virus has been reported in rural areas of\nthe tropical rain forests of west and central Africa, especially in the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo. The signs and symptoms are similar to those\nof smallpox, with generalized pustular rash, fever, and toxemia. The\ndisease, which is rare, became apparent only after the eradication of\nsmallpox. Monkeypox virus is a zoonotic agent and is acquired by humans\nthrough direct contact with the presumptive natural hosts: squirrels and\nmonkeys. Person-to-person transmission has been recorded, but the secondary\nattack rate is low. Nonetheless, the fear has been expressed that monkeypox\nmight evolve to become an endemic human infection. Determination of the\ncomplete sequence of the genome of monkeypox virus, however, has indicated\nthat monkeypox virus lacks some of the immunomodulatory genes possessed by\nsmallpox virus, which suggests that monkeypox virus is unlikely to become\nendemic in the human population.\nIn a comment relating to an outbreak in the same region of the Democratic\nRepublic of Congo in the year 2000 (see: Monkeypox - Congo, Dem. Rep.\n(Mbuji-Mayi): comment 20000506.069), Francois Merouze wrote that:\n\"Monkeypox is a zoonosis among African tree squirrels (_Heliosciurus_,\n_Funisciurus_). Human cases happen by direct contacts with sick animals.\nHuman to human subsequent cases are not very frequent. The usual mode for\nthis disease is anademic. There is no really prove[n] example of an\nimportant outbreak of monkeypox. The clinical diagnosis of monkeypox is not\nso easy, and chickenpox can be mistaken for monkeypox. Moreover, varicella\n(chickenpox), which is considered as an ordinary mild disease, can wear a\nvery different clinical aspect and even be a fatal disease for an\nimmunodepressed organism. In DRC, the prevalence rate of HIV is quite high.\"\nIt would be relevant to know whether chickenpox virus (a herpesvirus) has\nbeen excluded as the possible cause of this outbreak, since chickenpox is a\nvaccine-preventable disease. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (03) 20030710.1687\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (02): confirmed 20030626.1573\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep: suspected 20030623.1545\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (15) 20030710.1692\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem. All\nthese victims are from Kanama in the Kabinda District of the central region\nof the Democratic Republic of Congo.\n--\nShirley Bannatyne\nMASTA (Medical Advisory Service for Travellers Abroad)\n<shirley.bannatyne@masta.org>\n[Human infection with monkeypox virus has been reported in rural areas of\nthe tropical rain forests of west and central Africa, especially in the\nDemocratic Republic of Congo. The signs and symptoms are similar to those\nof smallpox, with generalized pustular rash, fever, and toxemia. The\ndisease, which is rare, became apparent only after the eradication of\nsmallpox. Monkeypox virus is a zoonotic agent and is acquired by humans\nthrough direct contact with the presumptive natural hosts: squirrels and\nmonkeys. Person-to-person transmission has been recorded, but the secondary\nattack rate is low. Nonetheless, the fear has been expressed that monkeypox\nmight evolve to become an endemic human infection. Determination of the\ncomplete sequence of the genome of monkeypox virus, however, has indicated\nthat monkeypox virus lacks some of the immunomodulatory genes possessed by\nsmallpox virus, which suggests that monkeypox virus is unlikely to become\nendemic in the human population.\nIn a comment relating to an outbreak in the same region of the Democratic\nRepublic of Congo in the year 2000 (see: Monkeypox - Congo, Dem. Rep.\n(Mbuji-Mayi): comment 20000506.069), Francois Merouze wrote that:\n\"Monkeypox is a zoonosis among African tree squirrels (_Heliosciurus_,\n_Funisciurus_). Human cases happen by direct contacts with sick animals.\nHuman to human subsequent cases are not very frequent. The usual mode for\nthis disease is anademic. There is no really prove[n] example of an\nimportant outbreak of monkeypox. The clinical diagnosis of monkeypox is not\nso easy, and chickenpox can be mistaken for monkeypox. Moreover, varicella\n(chickenpox), which is considered as an ordinary mild disease, can wear a\nvery different clinical aspect and even be a fatal disease for an\nimmunodepressed organism. In DRC, the prevalence rate of HIV is quite high.\"\nIt would be relevant to know whether chickenpox virus (a herpesvirus) has\nbeen excluded as the possible cause of this outbreak, since chickenpox is a\nvaccine-preventable disease. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (03) 20030710.1687\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep (02): confirmed 20030626.1573\nMonkeypox, human - Congo Rep: suspected 20030623.1545\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (15) 20030710.1692\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem. The finding of so many mild cases\nand no deaths so far is surprising. Some have commented that it is the\n\"comorbidity\" present in Africa that enhances the impact of monkeypox. I\nfind this explanation to be facile; there is no evidence that any\nparticular disease impacts on the outcome of infection with the virus in\nAfrica and there is no evidence that concomitant malnutrition or other\ncondition is needed to make monkeypox a serious disease. This may be so,\nbut I don't know of any data.\nIn fact, the only cases of monkeypox I have seen were in pygmies in the mid\n1980s and the general health of the group was quite good, although the\nstate of the recovering children reminded me very much of that of persons\nrecovering from measles with the added visible lymphadenopathy. Which of\ncourse brings to mind another obvious explanation for the mild course in\nthe US. There is better supportive care. This doesn't wash because the\ncases seem to be quite mild and not snatched from the jaws of death by\nnursing, antipyretics, rehydrations, and antibiotic treatment of secondary\ninfections as we attribute to differences in measles mortality.\nThere are, of course, many possible speculations including host and viral\ngenetics, recent passage in prairie dogs, etc. I would like to suggest a\nrather off-the-wall, but not unreasonable, idea that should be factored\ninto the equation: route of inoculation. This idea originates from the old\npractice of smallpox prevention by \"variolation\" or \"inoculation\". Smallpox\nvirus was introduced into the skin or nasal mucous membrane of a\nsusceptible person and the resulting disease was relatively mild and\nimmunized. If one reads the old English literature, one finds that the low\nmortality of about two per cent was obtained by a shallow inoculation in\nthe skin. The subsequent local cutaneous lesion appeared quickly and\napparently initiated a vigorous immune response that attenuated the disease\nwith the result of few pocks (appearing later) and mild host impact. This\nseems to fit some of the situations floating about on the rumor mill,\nalthough I have had no contact with these cases.\n--\nClarence J Peters\n<cjpeters@utmb.edu>\nSee Also\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (10): Kentucky 20030617.1498\nMonkeypox - European Union: prevention (import ban) 20030617.1497\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (09) 20030615.1479\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (08): NJ NOT 20030614.1470\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (07): transmi... 20030613.1458\nMonkeypox - USA: recommendations for vets (02) 20030613.1457\nMonkeypox - USA: recommendations for vets 20030612.1446\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (06) 20030612.1450\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(05) 20030611.1442\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem. This may be so,\nbut I don't know of any data.\nIn fact, the only cases of monkeypox I have seen were in pygmies in the mid\n1980s and the general health of the group was quite good, although the\nstate of the recovering children reminded me very much of that of persons\nrecovering from measles with the added visible lymphadenopathy. Which of\ncourse brings to mind another obvious explanation for the mild course in\nthe US. There is better supportive care. This doesn't wash because the\ncases seem to be quite mild and not snatched from the jaws of death by\nnursing, antipyretics, rehydrations, and antibiotic treatment of secondary\ninfections as we attribute to differences in measles mortality.\nThere are, of course, many possible speculations including host and viral\ngenetics, recent passage in prairie dogs, etc. I would like to suggest a\nrather off-the-wall, but not unreasonable, idea that should be factored\ninto the equation: route of inoculation. This idea originates from the old\npractice of smallpox prevention by \"variolation\" or \"inoculation\". Smallpox\nvirus was introduced into the skin or nasal mucous membrane of a\nsusceptible person and the resulting disease was relatively mild and\nimmunized. If one reads the old English literature, one finds that the low\nmortality of about two per cent was obtained by a shallow inoculation in\nthe skin. The subsequent local cutaneous lesion appeared quickly and\napparently initiated a vigorous immune response that attenuated the disease\nwith the result of few pocks (appearing later) and mild host impact. This\nseems to fit some of the situations floating about on the rumor mill,\nalthough I have had no contact with these cases.\n--\nClarence J Peters\n<cjpeters@utmb.edu>\nSee Also\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (10): Kentucky 20030617.1498\nMonkeypox - European Union: prevention (import ban) 20030617.1497\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (09) 20030615.1479\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (08): NJ NOT 20030614.1470\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (07): transmi... 20030613.1458\nMonkeypox - USA: recommendations for vets (02) 20030613.1457\nMonkeypox - USA: recommendations for vets 20030612.1446\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (06) 20030612.1450\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(05) 20030611.1442\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem. If one reads the old English literature, one finds that the low\nmortality of about two per cent was obtained by a shallow inoculation in\nthe skin. The subsequent local cutaneous lesion appeared quickly and\napparently initiated a vigorous immune response that attenuated the disease\nwith the result of few pocks (appearing later) and mild host impact. This\nseems to fit some of the situations floating about on the rumor mill,\nalthough I have had no contact with these cases.\n--\nClarence J Peters\n<cjpeters@utmb.edu>\nSee Also\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (10): Kentucky 20030617.1498\nMonkeypox - European Union: prevention (import ban) 20030617.1497\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (09) 20030615.1479\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (08): NJ NOT 20030614.1470\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (07): transmi... 20030613.1458\nMonkeypox - USA: recommendations for vets (02) 20030613.1457\nMonkeypox - USA: recommendations for vets 20030612.1446\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (06) 20030612.1450\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(05) 20030611.1442\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\n2002\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (07) 20021025.5638\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) (06) 20020410.3926\nMonkeypox - Congo DR (Equateur) 20020228.3654\n2001\n---\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur) (02) 20010927.2353\nMonkeypox, suspected - Congo DR (Equateur): RFI 20010315.0523\n2000\n---\nMonkeypox - Congo, Dem.
