For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-promed@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "smallpox", "monkeypox"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-10 00:00:00", "2021-03-08 00:00:00", "2021-03-07 00:00:00", "2002-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "1998-03-16 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Democratic Republic", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "US", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kentucky", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2204000", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Monkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (10): Kentucky", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2204000", "date": "2003-06-12 23:50:00", "main_text": "MONKEYPOX \u00ad- USA: RECOMMENDATIONS FOR VETERINARIANS\n**************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 11 Jun 2003\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: CDC website 11 Jun 2003 [edited]\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/monkeypox/animalguidance.htm>\n\n\nMonkeypox Infections In Animals: Interim Guidance for Veterinarians and Pet\nOwners\n------\nThe Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) has been working\nclosely with state and local health department and other partners to\ninvestigate cases of monkeypox virus infection among humans who had direct\nor close contact with recently purchased ill prairie dogs, a Gambian\nrat, and a rabbit that was housed in a veterinary clinic with an ill\nprairie dog.\n\nAs of 7 Jun 2003, at least 6 persons with probable monkeypox infection have\nbeen reported to be veterinarians or staff at veterinary hospitals.\n\nHuman monkeypox is a rare zoonotic viral disease that occurs primarily in\nthe rain forest countries of central and west Africa. In humans, the\nillness produces a vesicular and pustular rash similar to that produced by\nsmallpox.\n\nThe incubation period from exposure to fever onset is about 12 days. In\nhumans, case-fatality ratios in Africa have ranged from 1 to 10 percent\n(for additional information about monkeypox, see\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/eid/vol7no3/hutin.htm>).\n\nThese interim guidelines will be updated as new information becomes\navailable and after consultation with additional public health partners.\n\nIn the current outbreak, illness in animals has been reported to include\nfever, cough, blepharoconjunctivitis, lymphadenopathy, followed by a\nnodular rash. Some animals have died while others reportedly recovered.\n\nThe types of animals that may become ill with monkeypox are currently\nunknown; as a precaution, all mammals should be considered susceptible at\nthis time.\n\nPet owners who suspect their animal may have an illness compatible with\nmonkeypox should immediately isolate the animal from humans and other\nanimals and contact their state or local health department. In most cases,\nevaluation by a veterinarian will be recommended. Owners should notify the\nveterinarian before transporting the animal to the clinic so that\nappropriate infection control precautions can be implemented prior to arrival.\n\nOwners should consider wearing a mask and gloves when handling the animal.\nThe animal\u0092s bedding should be collected and bagged and brought with the\nanimal to the veterinary clinic for disposal as medically regulated waste.\n\nSoiled bedding should not be disposed of as household trash. Following\nidentification of an animal with suspect monkeypox in a household, any\nanimals (including dogs and cats) who may have come in contact with the\nsick animal should be closely observed for signs of illness for a period of\n1 month following the last date of exposure. Contaminated household\nsurfaces should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nTransport of the ill animal to a veterinary clinic should be done by a\nsingle individual to limit unnecessary exposure. At the time of animal\ntransport, the owner should also transport the sick animal\u0092s bagged bedding\nfor disposal. Care should be taken to minimize driver exposure during\ntransport. During transport animals should be confined in a closed\ncontainer such as a cardboard box. If available, the animal should be\nconfined in a separate compartment of the vehicle, such as the bed of a\npickup truck. Following transport, the area of the vehicle where the animal\nwas confined should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with the manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nVeterinarians examining sick prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or other\nmammals that may have come in contact with a suspected monkeypox case,\nshould consider the diagnosis of monkeypox infection. Animals that\ncurrently appear healthy but have been implicated as a probable source of\ninfection for a human case should also be treated as a suspected case of\nmonkeypox. All suspected cases of monkeypox in animals should be reported\nimmediately to state or local health department officials.\n\nVeterinarians who have been notified of an arriving animal with suspected\nmonkeypox should admit the animal through a separate entrance to a private\nroom and place it in isolation; the animal should not be admitted to an\nopen waiting room.\n\nWhen examining or euthanizing sick rodents, lagomorphs, and exotic pets,\nespecially prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or any ill mammal known to\nhave been in contact with a case of monkeypox, the following precautions\nshould be used:\n\n* Hand hygiene after all contact with a sick animal and contaminated surfaces.\n* Use of gown and gloves for any contact with the sick animal and\ncontaminated surfaces.\n* Eye protection (e.g. goggles or face shield) if splash or spray of body\nfluids is likely.\n* Respiratory protection including a NIOSH-certified N95 filtering\ndisposable respirator for entering the exam room. If N95 respirators are\nnot available for health-care personnel, then surgical masks should be worn.*\n* Contain and dispose of contaminated waste in accordance with\nfacility-specific guidelines for medically regulated infectious waste.\n* Handle used patient-care equipment in a manner that prevents\ncontamination of skin and clothing. Ensure that used equipment has been\ncleaned and reprocessed appropriately.\n* Ensure that procedures are in place for cleaning and disinfecting\ncontaminated environmental surfaces. Any EPA-registered hospital\ndetergent-disinfectant currently used by health-care facilities for\nenvironmental sanitation may be used. Manufacturer\u0092s recommendations for\ndilution (i.e., concentration), contact time, and care in handling should\nbe followed.\n* Laundry (e.g., towels, clothing) may be washed in a standard washing\nmachine with warm water and detergent; bleach may be added but is not\nnecessary. Care should be used when handling soiled laundry to avoid direct\ncontact with contaminated material. Soiled laundry should not be shaken or\notherwise handled in a manner that may aerosolize infectious particles.\n\nAll animals with suspected monkeypox infection should be humanely\neuthanized to prevent further spread of the disease. Humane euthanasia\noptions include pentobarbital injection or gas chamber using isoflurane or\nhalothane for most animals. For small rodent species such as mice, cervical\ndislocation may be considered if performed by a trained person.\n\nCDC is not currently recommending euthanasia of asymptomatic animals that\nmay have been exposed to other infected animals. Exposed asymptomatic\nanimals should be confined to the home and not allowed to come into contact\nwith other animals. They should be observed for development of signs\ncompatible with monkeypox for one month. Should such signs develop, the\nanimal should then be evaluated and euthanized if indicated.\n\nCurrently, CDC is not routinely requesting animal samples for testing.\nHowever, specimens can be collected and stored for possible future testing.\n\nConsultation with the state epidemiologist and state health laboratory is\nnecessary for samples to be sent to CDC for testing.\n<http://www.cste.org/members/state_and_territorial_epi.asp>\n<http://www.aphl.org/public_health_labs/index.cfm>\n\nPrior to euthanasia, whole blood in EDTA tubes or serum can be collected\nand refrigerated at 4 deg C. CDC is not recommending necropsies of animals\nexcept in Biosafety level 3 laboratories by persons recently vaccinated\nwith vaccinia. Instead, the entire carcass may be preserved by freezing at\n\u00ad70 deg C, or submersing in 10 percent formalin and injecting the body\ncavity with as much formalin as it will hold.\n\nFor more information, contact your state or local health department or the\nCDC Emergency Operations Center 770-488-7100. Additional information and\nrecommendations will be released as they become available. Updated\ninformation will be available at\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/monkeypox>.\n\n* Respirators should be used in the context of a complete respiratory\nprotection program in accordance with OSHA regulations. This includes\ntraining and fit testing to ensure a proper seal between the respirator\u0092s\nsealing surface and the wearer\u0092s face. Detailed information on respirator\nprograms, including fit test procedures can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory>.\n\nWhere possible, a qualitative fit test should be conducted for N95\nrespirators; detailed information on fit testing can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory/oshafiles/fittesting1.html>.\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[see also;\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (05) 20030611.1442]\n.......................mpp/tg/pg/jw\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with the manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nVeterinarians examining sick prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or other\nmammals that may have come in contact with a suspected monkeypox case,\nshould consider the diagnosis of monkeypox infection. Animals that\ncurrently appear healthy but have been implicated as a probable source of\ninfection for a human case should also be treated as a suspected case of\nmonkeypox. All suspected cases of monkeypox in animals should be reported\nimmediately to state or local health department officials.\n\nVeterinarians who have been notified of an arriving animal with suspected\nmonkeypox should admit the animal through a separate entrance to a private\nroom and place it in isolation; the animal should not be admitted to an\nopen waiting room.\n\nWhen examining or euthanizing sick rodents, lagomorphs, and exotic pets,\nespecially prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or any ill mammal known to\nhave been in contact with a case of monkeypox, the following precautions\nshould be used:\n\n* Hand hygiene after all contact with a sick animal and contaminated surfaces.\n* Use of gown and gloves for any contact with the sick animal and\ncontaminated surfaces.\n* Eye protection (e.g. goggles or face shield) if splash or spray of body\nfluids is likely.\n* Respiratory protection including a NIOSH-certified N95 filtering\ndisposable respirator for entering the exam room. If N95 respirators are\nnot available for health-care personnel, then surgical masks should be worn.*\n* Contain and dispose of contaminated waste in accordance with\nfacility-specific guidelines for medically regulated infectious waste.\n* Handle used patient-care equipment in a manner that prevents\ncontamination of skin and clothing. Ensure that used equipment has been\ncleaned and reprocessed appropriately.\n* Ensure that procedures are in place for cleaning and disinfecting\ncontaminated environmental surfaces. Any EPA-registered hospital\ndetergent-disinfectant currently used by health-care facilities for\nenvironmental sanitation may be used. Manufacturer\u0092s recommendations for\ndilution (i.e., concentration), contact time, and care in handling should\nbe followed.\n* Laundry (e.g., towels, clothing) may be washed in a standard washing\nmachine with warm water and detergent; bleach may be added but is not\nnecessary. Care should be used when handling soiled laundry to avoid direct\ncontact with contaminated material. Soiled laundry should not be shaken or\notherwise handled in a manner that may aerosolize infectious particles.\n\nAll animals with suspected monkeypox infection should be humanely\neuthanized to prevent further spread of the disease. Humane euthanasia\noptions include pentobarbital injection or gas chamber using isoflurane or\nhalothane for most animals. For small rodent species such as mice, cervical\ndislocation may be considered if performed by a trained person.\n\nCDC is not currently recommending euthanasia of asymptomatic animals that\nmay have been exposed to other infected animals. Exposed asymptomatic\nanimals should be confined to the home and not allowed to come into contact\nwith other animals. They should be observed for development of signs\ncompatible with monkeypox for one month. Should such signs develop, the\nanimal should then be evaluated and euthanized if indicated.\n\nCurrently, CDC is not routinely requesting animal samples for testing.\nHowever, specimens can be collected and stored for possible future testing.\n\nConsultation with the state epidemiologist and state health laboratory is\nnecessary for samples to be sent to CDC for testing.\n<http://www.cste.org/members/state_and_territorial_epi.asp>\n<http://www.aphl.org/public_health_labs/index.cfm>\n\nPrior to euthanasia, whole blood in EDTA tubes or serum can be collected\nand refrigerated at 4 deg C. CDC is not recommending necropsies of animals\nexcept in Biosafety level 3 laboratories by persons recently vaccinated\nwith vaccinia. Instead, the entire carcass may be preserved by freezing at\n\u00ad70 deg C, or submersing in 10 percent formalin and injecting the body\ncavity with as much formalin as it will hold.\n\nFor more information, contact your state or local health department or the\nCDC Emergency Operations Center 770-488-7100. Additional information and\nrecommendations will be released as they become available. Updated\ninformation will be available at\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/monkeypox>.\n\n* Respirators should be used in the context of a complete respiratory\nprotection program in accordance with OSHA regulations. This includes\ntraining and fit testing to ensure a proper seal between the respirator\u0092s\nsealing surface and the wearer\u0092s face. Detailed information on respirator\nprograms, including fit test procedures can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory>.\n\nWhere possible, a qualitative fit test should be conducted for N95\nrespirators; detailed information on fit testing can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory/oshafiles/fittesting1.html>.\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[see also;\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (05) 20030611.1442]\n.......................mpp/tg/pg/jw\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. In humans, the\nillness produces a vesicular and pustular rash similar to that produced by\nsmallpox.\n\nThe incubation period from exposure to fever onset is about 12 days. In\nhumans, case-fatality ratios in Africa have ranged from 1 to 10 percent\n(for additional information about monkeypox, see\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/eid/vol7no3/hutin.htm>).\n\nThese interim guidelines will be updated as new information becomes\navailable and after consultation with additional public health partners.\n\nIn the current outbreak, illness in animals has been reported to include\nfever, cough, blepharoconjunctivitis, lymphadenopathy, followed by a\nnodular rash. Some animals have died while others reportedly recovered.\n\nThe types of animals that may become ill with monkeypox are currently\nunknown; as a precaution, all mammals should be considered susceptible at\nthis time.\n\nPet owners who suspect their animal may have an illness compatible with\nmonkeypox should immediately isolate the animal from humans and other\nanimals and contact their state or local health department. In most cases,\nevaluation by a veterinarian will be recommended. Owners should notify the\nveterinarian before transporting the animal to the clinic so that\nappropriate infection control precautions can be implemented prior to arrival.\n\nOwners should consider wearing a mask and gloves when handling the animal.\nThe animal\u0092s bedding should be collected and bagged and brought with the\nanimal to the veterinary clinic for disposal as medically regulated waste.\n\nSoiled bedding should not be disposed of as household trash. Following\nidentification of an animal with suspect monkeypox in a household, any\nanimals (including dogs and cats) who may have come in contact with the\nsick animal should be closely observed for signs of illness for a period of\n1 month following the last date of exposure. Contaminated household\nsurfaces should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nTransport of the ill animal to a veterinary clinic should be done by a\nsingle individual to limit unnecessary exposure. At the time of animal\ntransport, the owner should also transport the sick animal\u0092s bagged bedding\nfor disposal. Care should be taken to minimize driver exposure during\ntransport. During transport animals should be confined in a closed\ncontainer such as a cardboard box. If available, the animal should be\nconfined in a separate compartment of the vehicle, such as the bed of a\npickup truck. Following transport, the area of the vehicle where the animal\nwas confined should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with the manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nVeterinarians examining sick prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or other\nmammals that may have come in contact with a suspected monkeypox case,\nshould consider the diagnosis of monkeypox infection. Animals that\ncurrently appear healthy but have been implicated as a probable source of\ninfection for a human case should also be treated as a suspected case of\nmonkeypox. All suspected cases of monkeypox in animals should be reported\nimmediately to state or local health department officials.\n\nVeterinarians who have been notified of an arriving animal with suspected\nmonkeypox should admit the animal through a separate entrance to a private\nroom and place it in isolation; the animal should not be admitted to an\nopen waiting room.\n\nWhen examining or euthanizing sick rodents, lagomorphs, and exotic pets,\nespecially prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or any ill mammal known to\nhave been in contact with a case of monkeypox, the following precautions\nshould be used:\n\n* Hand hygiene after all contact with a sick animal and contaminated surfaces.\n* Use of gown and gloves for any contact with the sick animal and\ncontaminated surfaces.\n* Eye protection (e.g. goggles or face shield) if splash or spray of body\nfluids is likely.\n* Respiratory protection including a NIOSH-certified N95 filtering\ndisposable respirator for entering the exam room. If N95 respirators are\nnot available for health-care personnel, then surgical masks should be worn.*\n* Contain and dispose of contaminated waste in accordance with\nfacility-specific guidelines for medically regulated infectious waste.\n* Handle used patient-care equipment in a manner that prevents\ncontamination of skin and clothing. Ensure that used equipment has been\ncleaned and reprocessed appropriately.\n* Ensure that procedures are in place for cleaning and disinfecting\ncontaminated environmental surfaces. Any EPA-registered hospital\ndetergent-disinfectant currently used by health-care facilities for\nenvironmental sanitation may be used. Manufacturer\u0092s recommendations for\ndilution (i.e., concentration), contact time, and care in handling should\nbe followed.\n* Laundry (e.g., towels, clothing) may be washed in a standard washing\nmachine with warm water and detergent; bleach may be added but is not\nnecessary. Care should be used when handling soiled laundry to avoid direct\ncontact with contaminated material. Soiled laundry should not be shaken or\notherwise handled in a manner that may aerosolize infectious particles.\n\nAll animals with suspected monkeypox infection should be humanely\neuthanized to prevent further spread of the disease. Humane euthanasia\noptions include pentobarbital injection or gas chamber using isoflurane or\nhalothane for most animals. For small rodent species such as mice, cervical\ndislocation may be considered if performed by a trained person.\n\nCDC is not currently recommending euthanasia of asymptomatic animals that\nmay have been exposed to other infected animals. Exposed asymptomatic\nanimals should be confined to the home and not allowed to come into contact\nwith other animals. They should be observed for development of signs\ncompatible with monkeypox for one month. Should such signs develop, the\nanimal should then be evaluated and euthanized if indicated.\n\nCurrently, CDC is not routinely requesting animal samples for testing.\nHowever, specimens can be collected and stored for possible future testing.\n\nConsultation with the state epidemiologist and state health laboratory is\nnecessary for samples to be sent to CDC for testing.\n<http://www.cste.org/members/state_and_territorial_epi.asp>\n<http://www.aphl.org/public_health_labs/index.cfm>\n\nPrior to euthanasia, whole blood in EDTA tubes or serum can be collected\nand refrigerated at 4 deg C. CDC is not recommending necropsies of animals\nexcept in Biosafety level 3 laboratories by persons recently vaccinated\nwith vaccinia. Instead, the entire carcass may be preserved by freezing at\n\u00ad70 deg C, or submersing in 10 percent formalin and injecting the body\ncavity with as much formalin as it will hold.\n\nFor more information, contact your state or local health department or the\nCDC Emergency Operations Center 770-488-7100. Additional information and\nrecommendations will be released as they become available. Updated\ninformation will be available at\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/monkeypox>.\n\n* Respirators should be used in the context of a complete respiratory\nprotection program in accordance with OSHA regulations. This includes\ntraining and fit testing to ensure a proper seal between the respirator\u0092s\nsealing surface and the wearer\u0092s face. Detailed information on respirator\nprograms, including fit test procedures can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory>.\n\nWhere possible, a qualitative fit test should be conducted for N95\nrespirators; detailed information on fit testing can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory/oshafiles/fittesting1.html>.\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[see also;\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (05) 20030611.1442]\n.......................mpp/tg/pg/jw\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. In humans, the\nillness produces a vesicular and pustular rash similar to that produced by\nsmallpox.\n\nThe incubation period from exposure to fever onset is about 12 days. In\nhumans, case-fatality ratios in Africa have ranged from 1 to 10 percent\n(for additional information about monkeypox, see\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/eid/vol7no3/hutin.htm>).\n\nThese interim guidelines will be updated as new information becomes\navailable and after consultation with additional public health partners.\n\nIn the current outbreak, illness in animals has been reported to include\nfever, cough, blepharoconjunctivitis, lymphadenopathy, followed by a\nnodular rash. Some animals have died while others reportedly recovered.\n\nThe types of animals that may become ill with monkeypox are currently\nunknown; as a precaution, all mammals should be considered susceptible at\nthis time.\n\nPet owners who suspect their animal may have an illness compatible with\nmonkeypox should immediately isolate the animal from humans and other\nanimals and contact their state or local health department. In most cases,\nevaluation by a veterinarian will be recommended. Owners should notify the\nveterinarian before transporting the animal to the clinic so that\nappropriate infection control precautions can be implemented prior to arrival.\n\nOwners should consider wearing a mask and gloves when handling the animal.\nThe animal\u0092s bedding should be collected and bagged and brought with the\nanimal to the veterinary clinic for disposal as medically regulated waste.\n\nSoiled bedding should not be disposed of as household trash. Following\nidentification of an animal with suspect monkeypox in a household, any\nanimals (including dogs and cats) who may have come in contact with the\nsick animal should be closely observed for signs of illness for a period of\n1 month following the last date of exposure. Contaminated household\nsurfaces should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nTransport of the ill animal to a veterinary clinic should be done by a\nsingle individual to limit unnecessary exposure. At the time of animal\ntransport, the owner should also transport the sick animal\u0092s bagged bedding\nfor disposal. Care should be taken to minimize driver exposure during\ntransport. During transport animals should be confined in a closed\ncontainer such as a cardboard box. If available, the animal should be\nconfined in a separate compartment of the vehicle, such as the bed of a\npickup truck. Following transport, the area of the vehicle where the animal\nwas confined should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with the manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nVeterinarians examining sick prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or other\nmammals that may have come in contact with a suspected monkeypox case,\nshould consider the diagnosis of monkeypox infection. Animals that\ncurrently appear healthy but have been implicated as a probable source of\ninfection for a human case should also be treated as a suspected case of\nmonkeypox. All suspected cases of monkeypox in animals should be reported\nimmediately to state or local health department officials.\n\nVeterinarians who have been notified of an arriving animal with suspected\nmonkeypox should admit the animal through a separate entrance to a private\nroom and place it in isolation; the animal should not be admitted to an\nopen waiting room.\n\nWhen examining or euthanizing sick rodents, lagomorphs, and exotic pets,\nespecially prairie dogs, Gambian giant rats, or any ill mammal known to\nhave been in contact with a case of monkeypox, the following precautions\nshould be used:\n\n* Hand hygiene after all contact with a sick animal and contaminated surfaces.\n* Use of gown and gloves for any contact with the sick animal and\ncontaminated surfaces.\n* Eye protection (e.g. goggles or face shield) if splash or spray of body\nfluids is likely.\n* Respiratory protection including a NIOSH-certified N95 filtering\ndisposable respirator for entering the exam room. If N95 respirators are\nnot available for health-care personnel, then surgical masks should be worn.*\n* Contain and dispose of contaminated waste in accordance with\nfacility-specific guidelines for medically regulated infectious waste.\n* Handle used patient-care equipment in a manner that prevents\ncontamination of skin and clothing. Ensure that used equipment has been\ncleaned and reprocessed appropriately.\n* Ensure that procedures are in place for cleaning and disinfecting\ncontaminated environmental surfaces. Any EPA-registered hospital\ndetergent-disinfectant currently used by health-care facilities for\nenvironmental sanitation may be used. Manufacturer\u0092s recommendations for\ndilution (i.e., concentration), contact time, and care in handling should\nbe followed.\n* Laundry (e.g., towels, clothing) may be washed in a standard washing\nmachine with warm water and detergent; bleach may be added but is not\nnecessary. Care should be used when handling soiled laundry to avoid direct\ncontact with contaminated material. Soiled laundry should not be shaken or\notherwise handled in a manner that may aerosolize infectious particles.\n\nAll animals with suspected monkeypox infection should be humanely\neuthanized to prevent further spread of the disease. Humane euthanasia\noptions include pentobarbital injection or gas chamber using isoflurane or\nhalothane for most animals. For small rodent species such as mice, cervical\ndislocation may be considered if performed by a trained person.\n\nCDC is not currently recommending euthanasia of asymptomatic animals that\nmay have been exposed to other infected animals. Exposed asymptomatic\nanimals should be confined to the home and not allowed to come into contact\nwith other animals. They should be observed for development of signs\ncompatible with monkeypox for one month. Should such signs develop, the\nanimal should then be evaluated and euthanized if indicated.\n\nCurrently, CDC is not routinely requesting animal samples for testing.\nHowever, specimens can be collected and stored for possible future testing.\n\nConsultation with the state epidemiologist and state health laboratory is\nnecessary for samples to be sent to CDC for testing.\n<http://www.cste.org/members/state_and_territorial_epi.asp>\n<http://www.aphl.org/public_health_labs/index.cfm>\n\nPrior to euthanasia, whole blood in EDTA tubes or serum can be collected\nand refrigerated at 4 deg C. CDC is not recommending necropsies of animals\nexcept in Biosafety level 3 laboratories by persons recently vaccinated\nwith vaccinia. Instead, the entire carcass may be preserved by freezing at\n\u00ad70 deg C, or submersing in 10 percent formalin and injecting the body\ncavity with as much formalin as it will hold.\n\nFor more information, contact your state or local health department or the\nCDC Emergency Operations Center 770-488-7100. Additional information and\nrecommendations will be released as they become available. Updated\ninformation will be available at\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/monkeypox>.\n\n* Respirators should be used in the context of a complete respiratory\nprotection program in accordance with OSHA regulations. This includes\ntraining and fit testing to ensure a proper seal between the respirator\u0092s\nsealing surface and the wearer\u0092s face. Detailed information on respirator\nprograms, including fit test procedures can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory>.\n\nWhere possible, a qualitative fit test should be conducted for N95\nrespirators; detailed information on fit testing can be accessed at:\n<http://www.osha.gov/sltc/etools/respiratory/oshafiles/fittesting1.html>.\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[see also;\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (WI, IL, IN) 20030608.1412\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(02) 20030609.1422\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(03) 20030610.1433\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA(WI,IL,IN)(04) 20030611.1436\nMonkeypox, human, prairie dogs - USA (05) 20030611.1442]\n.......................mpp/tg/pg/jw\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. In humans, the\nillness produces a vesicular and pustular rash similar to that produced by\nsmallpox.\n\nThe incubation period from exposure to fever onset is about 12 days. In\nhumans, case-fatality ratios in Africa have ranged from 1 to 10 percent\n(for additional information about monkeypox, see\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/eid/vol7no3/hutin.htm>).\n\nThese interim guidelines will be updated as new information becomes\navailable and after consultation with additional public health partners.\n\nIn the current outbreak, illness in animals has been reported to include\nfever, cough, blepharoconjunctivitis, lymphadenopathy, followed by a\nnodular rash. Some animals have died while others reportedly recovered.\n\nThe types of animals that may become ill with monkeypox are currently\nunknown; as a precaution, all mammals should be considered susceptible at\nthis time.\n\nPet owners who suspect their animal may have an illness compatible with\nmonkeypox should immediately isolate the animal from humans and other\nanimals and contact their state or local health department. In most cases,\nevaluation by a veterinarian will be recommended. Owners should notify the\nveterinarian before transporting the animal to the clinic so that\nappropriate infection control precautions can be implemented prior to arrival.\n\nOwners should consider wearing a mask and gloves when handling the animal.\nThe animal\u0092s bedding should be collected and bagged and brought with the\nanimal to the veterinary clinic for disposal as medically regulated waste.\n\nSoiled bedding should not be disposed of as household trash. Following\nidentification of an animal with suspect monkeypox in a household, any\nanimals (including dogs and cats) who may have come in contact with the\nsick animal should be closely observed for signs of illness for a period of\n1 month following the last date of exposure. Contaminated household\nsurfaces should be cleaned and disinfected. Standard household\ncleaning/disinfectants may be used in accordance with manufacturer\u0092s\ninstructions.\n\nTransport of the ill animal to a veterinary clinic should be done by a\nsingle individual to limit unnecessary exposure. At the time of animal\ntransport, the owner should also transport the sick animal\u0092s bagged bedding\nfor disposal. Care should be taken to minimize driver exposure during\ntransport. Any EPA-registered hospital\ndetergent-disinfectant currently used by health-care facilities for\nenvironmental sanitation may be used. Manufacturer\u0092s recommendations for\ndilution (i.e., concentration), contact time, and care in handling should\nbe followed.\n* Laundry (e.g., towels, clothing) may be washed in a standard washing\nmachine with warm water and detergent; bleach may be added but is not\nnecessary. Care should be used when handling soiled laundry to avoid direct\ncontact with contaminated material. Soiled laundry should not be shaken or\notherwise handled in a manner that may aerosolize infectious particles.\n\nAll animals with suspected monkeypox infection should be humanely\neuthanized to prevent further spread of the disease. Humane euthanasia\noptions include pentobarbital injection or gas chamber using isoflurane or\nhalothane for most animals. A new drug may work against\nmonkeypox, a relative of smallpox that has caused a worrying outbreak in\nAfrica, health experts said on Tuesday. Researchers from the U.S. Army\nMedical Research Institute of Infectious Diseases (USAMRIID) said the drug,\ncidofovir, made by California-based Gilead Sciences Inc. (GILD.O), prevented\ndeath and disease in animals infected with monkeypox virus. Cidofovir\nreduced respiratory symptoms, fever and pox-like skin lesions, said Dr. John\nHuggins, who heads viral therapeutics at USAMRIID. \"This is the first time\nwe have data in primates that confirm a drug's potential activity against\northopoxvirus infections,\" Huggins told an infectious diseases meeting\nsponsored by the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC).\n\"The disease observed in primates infected with monkeypox is clinically\nsimilar to what occurs in humans,\" he added. The next step would be to see\nif the drug might work in humans. More than 500 people in the Democratic\nRepublic of Congo became ill in the largest ever reported outbreak of human\nmonkeypox last year. Five people, aged between 4 and 8, died. Monkeypox is a\nsister virus of smallpox and is generally spread by squirrels and monkeys\nin the rain forests of western and central Africa. Before the outbreak in\nthe Democratic Republic of Congo, cases of monkeypox in humans were rare.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n.......................................mhj/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. A new drug may work against\nmonkeypox, a relative of smallpox that has caused a worrying outbreak in\nAfrica, health experts said on Tuesday. Researchers from the U.S. Army\nMedical Research Institute of Infectious Diseases (USAMRIID) said the drug,\ncidofovir, made by California-based Gilead Sciences Inc. (GILD.O), prevented\ndeath and disease in animals infected with monkeypox virus. Cidofovir\nreduced respiratory symptoms, fever and pox-like skin lesions, said Dr. John\nHuggins, who heads viral therapeutics at USAMRIID. \"This is the first time\nwe have data in primates that confirm a drug's potential activity against\northopoxvirus infections,\" Huggins told an infectious diseases meeting\nsponsored by the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC).\n\"The disease observed in primates infected with monkeypox is clinically\nsimilar to what occurs in humans,\" he added. The next step would be to see\nif the drug might work in humans. More than 500 people in the Democratic\nRepublic of Congo became ill in the largest ever reported outbreak of human\nmonkeypox last year. Five people, aged between 4 and 8, died. Monkeypox is a\nsister virus of smallpox and is generally spread by squirrels and monkeys\nin the rain forests of western and central Africa. Before the outbreak in\nthe Democratic Republic of Congo, cases of monkeypox in humans were rare.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n.......................................mhj/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Increased activity of monkeypox was reported in Katako-Kombe health\nzone, Sankuru sub-region, Kasai Oriental during 1996 (see Weekly\nEpidemiological Record No.43, 25 October 1996). Medecins Sans Frontieres\ninvestigated those cases which could be traced in September 1996. Samples\nwere obtained from 11 cases all of which were subsequently confirmed as\nmonkeypox by the WHO Collaborating Centre for Smallpox and other Poxvirus\nInfections at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in\nAtlanta, USA. Concern about the unusual high number of cases and the\napparent increased transmission between human cases triggered a\nmultidisciplinary investigation in February 1997. A team was set up composed\nof national experts, local WHO staff, staff from CDC, Atlanta, USA and\nEpiet, Paris, France.\nThe report of the investigations will be published simultaneously in\nforthcoming issues of the Weekly Epidemiological Record, the Morbidity and\nMortality Weekly Report and the Eurosurveillance bulletin.\n..............................................mhj\n\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
